II. THE BENEFICIARIES
Introduction
2.01     The two principal beneficiaries of the project are the Government
of Maharashtra Irrigation Department (GOMID), which vill implement the
Koyna IV hydroelectric component, and the Maharashtra State Electricity
Board (MSEB). MSEB vill have responsibility for implementation of the
Transmission and Distribution component, as well as for the operation and
maintenance of Koyna IV hydro component (under a 30-year leasing
arrangement with GOM) once construction completed. A brief account of the
two Action Plans, aimed respectively at trengthening the operational
management as well as consolidating the longer run financial soundness of
MSEB, is given in paras 2.12 and 4.01.
A. Government of Maharashtra Irrigation Department
2.02     GOMID is an operational unit of the Government of Maharashtra,
responsible for implementing all irrigation and hydropower schemes in
Maharashtra. The Bank au.d GOMID have been associated in a number of
irrigation and hydroelectric projects dating back to the earlier Koyna
developments. The most recent project financed by the Bank and implemented
by GOMID is the Maharashtra Composite Irrigation Project (Loan 1621-IN),
approved in 1986. GOMID is therefore thoroughly familiar with the Bank's
procedures for project implementation.
Organization and Technical Capabilities
2.03     Civil engineering design is carried out under the chief engineer
(projects). He is responsible for the Central Design Office (CDO) which
carries out major civil design work; he is also responsible for the
Maharashtra Engineering Research Institute (a well reputed organization
carrying out basic and applied research in the civil engineering field),
the Dam Safety Organization, and the Engineering Staff College. The chief
engineer (electrical) is responsible for the design of permanent equipment
for each installation, and the chief engineer (mechanical) is responsible
for provision and maintenance of construction plant by force account units.
Coordination of the various inputs is the responsibility of the regional
superintending engineer in charge of the project. Improvement to the
existing organization will be implemented under the proposed project
(para 3.10).
2.04     At the operating level, responsibility is divided among a number
of functional divisions and six regional divisions, each headed by a chief
engineer. Regional chief engineers are responsible for investigation and
construction of new projects and operation and maintenance of existing
irrigation projects.
2.05     GOMID's design and construction organization is well established
and experienced.  GOID and its predecessor (Maharashtra Izrigation and
Power Department) have been responsible for the construction of some 600
large and medium-sized dams throughout the state. GOMID also has
considerable experience in hydropower, having installed some 1,200 MW over



-17-
the last 30 years. Most of their hydro developments are small to medium
sized plants associated with irrigation dams. However, they have also been
responsible for a number of large developments, including the first three
stages of the Koyna scheme, which are directly relevant to the currently
proposed development. Stages I and II, which were completed in 1967, have
an installed capacity of 560 MW in eight units, and a very similar
configuration to Stage TV. The Stage III development, completed in 1976,
also inclu.des a major dam, an underground powerhouse, long tunnels, and a
further 500 MW of capacity in four units. In the construction area, the
practice of GOMID carrying out a large proportion of its work by force
account has resulted in the desirable situation of supervising engineers
having direct construction experience. Specific GOMID arrangements to
implement Koyna IV are discussed in Chapter III.
2.06     Hydropower plants in Maharashtra belong to GOM; they are leased
for 30 years to MSEB, which is fully responsible for their operation . i
maintenance. For each plant, the lease rent payment is calculated so that
GOM recoup all investment costs for power generation, at an 8Z interest
rate, over a 30-year period. After 30 years, the facility will return to
GOM and a new lease contract would be negotiated. All hydro plants in
Maharishtra are less than 30 years old, but the lease for the older ones
will be eligible for renegotiation within two years. MsEB bears all
operation and maintenance costs.
2.07     The 8Z interest rate used for calculation of the lease-rent
payments is substantially below the ongoing interest rate for long-term
loans to MSEB (1lZ to 14Z), and the rate charged by GOM for loans to its
agencies and corporations (9Z). Hence, it arguably includes an element of
subsidization. Moreover, the 30-year period used in the calculation is
less than the economic life of most hydro plants. This situation should be
corrected in the future for new hydro plants; hence under the Financial
Action Plan (Annex 4.4, para 2(c)), GOM and MSEB will revise the terms of
the lease agreements to be signed later than December 31, 1989.
Project Accounts and Audit
2.08     External audit of project accounts will be carried out by the
Comptroller and Auditor General of India, through his local
representatives, the Auditor General of Maharashtra. Agreement was reached
with GOM during negotiations that GOMID will set up separate accounts for
the proposed project, to be audited annually by an independent auditor
acceptable to the Bank, and that it will provide the Bank with audited
financial statements of said project accounts within nine months of the end
of each fiscal year, together with a certified report by the Auditor.
B. Maharashtra State Electricity Board
Introduction
2.09     The proposed project would be the second Bank operation with MSEB.
The first project (Chandrapur Thermal Power Project, Loan 2544-IN) focussed
on training, reduction of transmission and distribution losses, financial
accounting, and tariff structure improvement. The project is proceeding



-18-
satisfactorily, except with regard to commercial receivables (paras 4.06
and 4.17), and delay in preparation of a tariff structure study now
underway (para 2.17).
2.10     MSEB was constituted in 1954 under the Electricity (Supply) Act of
1948; it is responsible for generation, transmission, and distribution of
electricity throughout the State of Maharashtra. It constitutes the
largest state power network in India, with an installed capacity of about
6,000 MW as of end-1987 (13Z of all-India); 256,000 km of distribution
lines, and 150,000 km of above 11 kV transmission lines. It has about
97,000 employees (10Z of the sector). MSEB seives about 6.5 million
connections comprising 4.3 million domestic, 0.7 million commercial, 0.2
million industrial, 1.2 million agric__tura)., and less than 0.1 million
miscellaneous. Industrial consumption represents 38Z of the total, while
commercial represents 2Z; agriculture 17Z; domestic consumption 8Z; and
other consumers and bulk power trading 35X. The Board constructs and
operates generating stations and transmission and distribution networks to
supply final consumers, and also issues licenses to private power utilities
in Maharashtra 9/ and for private captive generation. MSEB is not fully
autonomous in executing its responsibilities, since the Government of
Maharashtra Energy Department (GOMED) exercises considerable infl-ence over
the Board's activities, especially in matters of staffing, borrowing, and
tariff-setting. Furthermore, MSEB's capital investment program is an
integral part of Mahareshtra's capital budget formulated by GOI ntd GOM on
a five-year basis.
2.11     Although there is scope for improvement in management efficiency
and productivity by international standards (Annex 2.1) as well as for
improved financial performance, MSEB raiks favourably in comparison with
other SEBs in Ii.dia (Annex 2.2) in terms of efficiency and financial
performance; it is the seventh SEB in terms of number of employees per
1,000 consumers (17.5 employees/l,000 consumers), and the second for the
number of employees per GWh (4.6 employees per GWh); it was one of the two
SEBs in the country with a positive net income in FY 1986/87, and a rate of
return (on historically valued net fixed assets, after depreciation,
interest and taxes) close to the 32 minimum stipulated in the Electricity
(Supply) Act (equivalent to a 9.3Z ROR on historical assets according to
Bank definition).
2.12     During preparation of the proposed project, the management,
operations and finances of MSEB have been reviewed in the context of an
Action Plan for Managerial Development (.nnex 2.3), and a Financial Action
Plan (A ,ex 4.4), aimed at strengthening the overall operational,
managerial and financial performance of this utility. The main areas that
would need improvement are organization, management information systems,
and finances. MSEB's present organization was found to be generally
satisfactory. However, in order to improve the quality of electricity
service and enhance productivity, the organization of MSEB will need to be
strengthened in the following areas: (a) corporate planning; (b)
distribution planning; (c) operations performance monitoring; (d)
management accounting; (e) environment protection; and (f) management
9/ The Tata Electric Companies (TEC) and Bombay Suburban Electricity
Supply (BSES), both operating in Bombay.



-19-
information systems. The necessary actions needed to achieve this
improvement have been discussed with MSEB's management, and are included in
an Action Plan for Manager'al Development (Annex 2.3), including specific
targets, proposed actions to achieve them, and an implementation timetable.
The different aspects of the Action Plan are discussed below in the ensuing
paragraphs. During negotiations, agreement was reached with MSEB on the
content of the Action Plan as well as on its implementation in accordance
with an agreed timetable (para 6.02(a)). Regarding electricity tariffs,
specific actions to improve cost recovery from consumers and inter-state
power trading have already been identified and are discussed in para 2.17.
Measures necessary to improve the financial situation of MSEB and
strengthen its financial autonomy are discussed in Chapter IV.
Organization
2.13     MSEB is a corporate body managed by a Board consisting of a full-
time Chairman, and three full-time Members (for Accounts, Technical, and
Administration). Board Members are appointed by GOM, and define MSEB's
general policies, as well as managing their respective sectors. The Board
meets about twice every month. MSEB's field organization for distribution
(Annex 2.4) is generally satisfactory. It is set up around seven Regional
Distribution Zones, under a Technical Director (Distribution). Each Zone,
headed by a Regional Chief Engineer, comprises about five Circles, each of
fi-re Divisions. An average Division serves about 60,000 consumers, with a
workforce of about 700. The Division is a fairly autonomous entity, in
charge of distribution system maintenance, commercial relations with
consumers, billing and collection, consumers connection and disconnection,
local accounting, and personnel management. Generation (Operations &
Haintenance) is organized in the field in eight zones corresponding to the
main power plants and transmission substations. MSEB directly executes a
substantial part of its development program, espertally transmission lines
and substations, with its departmental organization.
Operation and Maintenance
2.14     MSEB's operation and plant maintenance standards and practices are
satisfactory. This is confirmed by the relative low load shedding taking
place in a system with little or no spare capacity. Overall plant
performance is acceptable. Major maintenance is done by contractors or
suppliers and routine maintenance is done by MSEB staff. Major equipment
is maintained in accordance with routines and schedules prescribed by
manufacturers. Transmiscion lines are inspected at least once a month, and
major components of distribution systems are also inspected periodically in
accordance with pre-established schedules. Reporting of performance for
generation and analysis of field reports are systematically done at MSEB's
headquarters. Reporting of performance for Transmission and most of all,
for Distribution is less satisfactory, where the management is not provided
with a consistent set of operational performance indicators. As part of
the Action Plan for Managerial Development (Annex 2.3, para 2.3), a
performance monitoring system will be implemented, and present monitoring
indicators will be reviewed and revised, if needed. Moreover, a central
Operation and Maintenance (O&M) unit will be established to monitor
technical performance indicators and maintenance standards (terms of
reference are given in Annex 2.3, Attachment 4). Training as well as



-20-
technical visits to selected public utilities to acquaint MSEB's staff with
on-going practices in this respect will be financed under the proposed
project (para 3.06). Maintenance of installations such as buildings,
auxiliary services (i.e. water treatment plants, repair shops, storage
area, etc.) is corrective rather than preventive. Standards are generally
below those for a utility of the size and importance of MSEB. The general
condition and cleanliness of some installation poses risks to safe plant
operation and to safety and health of staff. Under the Action Plan for
Managerial Development (Annex 2.3. para 2.3), MSEB will establish by
March 31, 1990, an O&M unit, and prepare, before December 31, 1990,
maintenance manuals and preventive maintenance routines.
Tariffs
2.15     MSEB's existing tariff is summarized in Annex 2.5, together with
the relationship of the main elements of the tariff in comparison with
estimated economic supply costs. The principal features of the tariff are
its complexity, the cross-subsidy of low voltage consumers (and
agricultural consumers in particular) by industry and the absence of any
distinction between the co'sts of providing energy during peak and off-peak
periods. MSEB's average tariff level of 75 paise per kWh in 1987188 is
estimated to have been equivalent to approximately 74Z of the marginal cost
of supplies and is one of the highest in India. However, the structure of
MSEB's tariff shows sharp divergences from the structure of marginal cost
estimates. For example, while the tariff to large industrial consumers is
equivalent to approximately 1152 of the marginal cost of supplying these
consumers, agricultural consumers are charged only 82 of the marginal cost
of their supplies. Similarly, the tariff to commercial consumers is
approximately 602 of marginal cost, while that to domestic consumers is
about only 24Z of marginal cost.
2.16     Under the 1985 revision to the Electricity (Supply) Act, SEBs have
the authority to adjust the level and structure of their tariffs. In
practice, however, SEBs do not adjust tariffs without the concurrence of
their state government; power tariffs (particularly to low voltage
consumers) have become a highly charged political issue. Nevertheless, the
pace of reform of MSEB's tariff has been impressive. Since 1984/85, the
average level of MSEB's tariff has increased 25.02 in real terms, an
average real increase of 7.7Z per annum (Annex 2.5), page 2). Prices to
industry increased over 30? in real terms during this period as did prices
to bulk purchasers of power. 10/
2.17     The proposed project includes actions which aim to continue
improving the level and structure of MSEB's tariff in relation to economic
supply costs through a series of load management initiatives and selective
tariff increases. This project component will be based upon the findings
and recommendations of a study of MSEB-s tariff presently being undertaken
by consultants. During negotiations, MSEB agreed that, prior to
December 31, 1989, it would furnish to the Bank a copy of the finsl report
of the tariff study (para 6.02(b)). It was understood during negotiations
that the Bank will provide MSEB with comments on the findings and
recommendations of the study, and that MSEB will take the Bank's comments
10/ MSEB also provides bulk power supplies to TEC, BSES and States in the
Western and Southern Regions.



-21-
into account when formulating its proposals for tariff adjustments and load
management initiatives. MSEB also agreed that, prior to June 30, 1990, it
will afford the Bank an opportunity to comment on the tariff adjustments
and load management initiatives it lntends to implement based, inter alia,
on the recommendation of the tariff study (para 6.02(b)).
Personnel and Staffing
2.18     At the end of FY1988, HSEB had about 97,000 regular employees, and
about 8,000 skilled workers employed in non-permanent positions
(Annex 2.6). About 6,000 of the regular workforce and all the daily wage
workers were engaged in construction activities. Distribution and
Operation & Maintenance staff represent 85,000 employees, of which more
than 50? were unskilled workers.  Although significant labor productivity
gains were achieved over the last few years (from 21 employees per thousand
consumers in FY83 to 15 employees per thousand consumers in FY1987/88), and
MSEB's labour productivity is one of the highest amongst India's State
Electricity Boards, MSEB is still over-staffed compared to public utilities
in cceparable developing countries (Annex 2.1) (for example, in Indonesia,
there are 81 consumers per employee). MSEB's management has placed, in FY
1986/87, a freeze on recruitment of all staff below junior enginseer level
(levels III and IV). Future expansion (7.7? per annum in terms of power
generation, and 6.2? per annum in terms of number of consumers) over the
FY 1987188 to FY 1994/95 period is expected to be met with an average
labour force increase of 2X per annum. To achieve this target, MSEB's
Action Plan for Managerial Development includes the preparation of a human
resources development plan, including monitorable annual staff productivity
targets, and a manpower plan (Annex 2.3, para 3.3, and Attachment 7).
Training
2.19     MSEB has an active Training Department located at Nasik,
supervising eight training centers (four for generation, and four for T&D),
mainly for technicians and engineers. In addition, MSEB has in-house
training programs in accounting and administration, and external training
programs in finance, management of human resources for higher level staff,
computer skills and science, and in specialized technical fields. In
FY1987/88, training courses were delivered to 1,329 employees in different
fields, of which 1,273 within MSEB (Annex 2.7). MSEB has also developed
human resources management and staff motivation programs, and is
experimenting--with some success--with Quality Circles and "protege"
(mentor) arrangements for junior staff. However, there is a deficiency in
specific training for higher level staff in public utilities management,
particularly in management accounting, corporate planning, operation
performance monitoring systems, and MIS computerization for public
utilities. The proposed project will finance specific training programs in
public utilities management and MIS (operation and maintenance, para 2.14;
financial planning, para 2.21; and management accounting, para 2.23).   The
Action Plan for Managerial Developmeit also includes specific targets for
computer training (Annex 2.3, para 3.2).



-22-
Corporate Planning
2.20     To date MSEB has no Investment Planning or Corporate Planning unit
to ptepare medium and long-term development plans and to integrate the
plans of the different organizational units. Investment Planning is
nominally under the Chief Engineer (Transmission Planning); Operation
Planning is coordinated by the Director Generation (Operations &
Maintenance); Sales Planning is under the Chief Engineer (Commercial);
Personnel Planning under the Member (Administration); and Financial
Planning is under the Director (Finance). The planning cycle in MSEB is
limited to the preparation every five years of the State and National
Plans. With the considerable developments planned to occur in the future,
coordination between generation, T&D, commercial, and finance functions is
becoming increasingly important. The planning function needs to be
reorganized and strengthened, to become a centralized, coordinating, and
forwarding-looking function. The need for a planning unit is recognized by
MSEB management, and the establishment of a centralized planning unit is
included in the Action Plan for Managerial Development (Annex 2.3, para 2.2
and Attachment 2). This unit will prepare and maintain a detailed,
revolving five-year plan, as well as long-term indicative 10 and 20-year
plans; it will also coordinate the preparation of disaggregated power
demand forecasts, and detailed plans for generation and T&D, investment,
finance, and manpower.
Financial Planning
2.21     Financial planning is carried out mainly for the annual
calculation and negotiation of GOM loans to MSEB, and the preparation of
the National and State Plans, but not for management purpose. The Board
prepares every five years, as part of the National and State planning
process, reports on its investment program and investment financing
requirements. These documents are of little practical use for financial
planning purpose, as they are prepared in constant prices, and do not
provide a complete investment and working capital financing plan including
all sources available. Moreover, the five-year plan is not updated
annually, and therefore rapidly becomes obsolete. An experimental
Financial Planning Unit has been established in relation with the
preparation of the proposed project, but its present staffing is
insufficient, and the scope for strengthening it is limited by the acute
shortage in MSEB of suitably trained staff. The unit also needs data
processing support, but the introduction of micro-computers and
computerized financial planning models is being delayed by the necessary
lead-time to train staff in their operation. The strengthening of the
Financial Planning Unit, and the satisfaction of its logistic needs is
included in the Action Plan for Managerial Development (Annex 2.3,
para 2.2). The proposed project will provide support for purchase of data
processing hardware and software, and for training abroad, as necessary
(para 3.06, and Annex 3.1).
Financial Organization and Accounting
2.22     Financial operations in MSEB are regulated by the Electricity
(Supply) Act, 1948, amended in 1978 and 1985 to reorganize finances of SEBs
along commercial lines. MSEB successfully introduced new commercial



-23-
accounting system in 1985. The organization of MSEB's financial complex is
satisfactory. It is is headed by a full-time Board Member (Accounts), who
is a senior member of the Indian Administrative Service. He is assisted by
a Director (Accounts) for financial accounting; a Director (Finance) for
revenue collection, financial operations, treasury, and the budget; a
Director (Management Accounts) for consumer billing, computer center
management, and management accounting; and a Director (Internal Audit) for
field office inspection, internal financial audit, and relations with the
External Auditor. In addition, the Accounting Department has field offices
in every zone and division, under the administrative authority of the local
Chief Engineer, but with permanent functional relations with the
Directorate of Finance and the Directorate of Management Accounts.
Accounts Department staff are competent and well-trained.
2.23     Management Accounting needs to be strengthened.  Reasonably
reliable cost data are available only for thermal generation, but not for
T&D. Data availability is insufficient for tariff setting, performance
monitoring, or economic analysis. MSEB is aware of this weakness and has
requested Bank support under the proposed project to modernize its
management accounting system. The proposed project includes provision for
organizing a training programlworkshop for upper level management
accounting staff (para 3.06 and Annex 3.1). The implementation of a new
management accounting system is included in the Action Plan for Managerial
Development (Annex 2.3, para 3.1), under terms of reference given in
Attachment 6 to Annex 2.3.
External Audit
2.24     The audit of MSEB's accounts is carried out annually by the
Comptroller and Auditor General of India through his local representative,
the Auditor General of Maharashtra. The FY1987/88 audit report was
completed recently and found satisfactory. Accounting procedures in MSEB
have been found satisfactory in general jy the Auditor. According to the
Act, the accounts and the Auditor's report must be submitted to the State
Government within six months of the close of the year. In the past, MSEB
has submitted the financial statements to the Auditor within the six-month
limit, but the Auditor's report was finalized after the six-month period,
although within the nine-month period stipulated in the Chandrapur Thermal
Power Project. The existing audit arrangements under the on-going
Chandrapur Thermal Power Project are satisfactory, and should be continued.
During negotiations, MSEB agreed to continue to furnish to the Bank, by no
later than nine months after the end of each fiscal year, its audited
accounts together with the Auditor's certificate and report.
Internal Audit
2.25     MSEB's internal control is limited at present to financial and
monetary transactions, including capital expenditures, store purchases,
billing and collection. Controls are carried out by the Chief Auditor, who
reports to the Member (Accounts). This unit has proved to be efficient in
establishment of sound financial procedures. However, its role is limited
to financial transactions, and does not extend to all managerial practices
and internal procedures, which are also covered in modern internal audit
units. MSoreover, the placement of this unit under the Member (Accounts)



-24-
may limit its autonomy and independence. The extension of the role of the
Internal Audit Unit, and suitable arrangements to guarantee its
independence and autonomy are included in the Action Plan for Managerial
Development (Annex 2.3, para 2.3), and terms of reference are given in
Attachment 5 to Annex 2.3.
Consumers Billing
2.26     All electricity consumption is metered, except for low voltage
agriculture. Meter reading and billing is done on a monthly basis for
industrial and agricultural high voltage consumers. For other consumers,
meter reading is carried out every two months (every four months in rural
areas). Although consumeL billing is still mostly manual, it is reasonably
efficient and is not responsible for collection delays. MSEB has initiated
in 1974 a billing computerization program, with one computer center in each
zone. To date four out of seven zones have been successfully computerized
(Pune, Bombay, Nagpur, and Aurangabad). Computerization of the remaining
three zones is delayed by the shortage of staff with computer skills
(despite MSEB's computer training program), the considerable work required
for the initial data input (each region represent about one million
consumers), and telecommunication deficiencies between regions. These
issues will be addressed under MSEB's Data Processing Plan (para 2.28).
Management Information System and Data Processing
2.27     MSEB has embarked on the development of its management information
system (MIS), and encouraging results have been achieved in the areas of
financial accounting, consumer billing, investment project monitoring,
inventory management and fixed assets management; the MIS is still under
development for personnel management.  At present, each of the main
departments has its own MIS and data processing systems which have been
developed independently.  With the growth !  the number of consumers, the
increased generation capacity, and the expansion of the transmission and
distribution network, an increasing volume of data must be collected and
processed to provide timely information to the decision centers.  MIS
development has not kept up to the Board's expansion, hence some systems
need to be modernized and integrated to become an effective management
tool. In consideration of the size of the organization, its limited
absorptive capacity and the shortage of managerial skills, MSEB has adopted
a piecemeal approach, progressing sub-system by sub-systam. Under the
Action Plan for Managerial Development, MSEB will redesign in priority the
Operations Management System and performance indicator monitoring system
(para 2.14), and management accounting system (para 2.23).
2.28     Computerization in MSEB was initiated in 1974.  However, it is
still at an infant stage. Until now, the Finance Department, who is the
heaviest user, has taken the lead for managerial computer development.
The pace of computerization hap been slow, because of restrictions on
acquisition of suitable hardwaLe and software, shortage of qualified staff,
and resistance from labour unions. However, the situation has improved,
since computer acquisition has been liberalized. MSEB has now a regular
computer training program, and resistance from unions has diminished. With
the acceleration of computerization, the need for coordination of the
initiative taken by the different departments has become more pressing.



-25-
Under the Action Plan for Managerial Development (Annex 2.3, paras 2.2,
and 3.2), MSEB will: (i) create a Data Processing department in charge of
mainframe computer operation, development of software for management, and
coordination of acquisition of data processing hardware and software; (ii)
accelerate training in data processing; and (iii) prepare a time-bound
long-term Data Processing plan, setting long term goals, and outlining
MSEB's proposed strategy (terms of reference are given in Annex 2.3,
Attachment 3).
Tax Regime
2.29     HSEB is liable for corporate taxes according to the Indian
corporate tax laws, which stipulate that corporate tax is collected by the
Central Government. In the past, GOI's investment financing policy for
MSEB has kept the interest burden on MSEB high, therefore the Board did not
show a taxable income. MSEB's accumulated losses and the accelerated
depreciation allowed for corporate tax purposes, which can be carried
forward, are such that MSEB will not incur any income tax obligation over
the next ten years, even at the targeted rate of return of 4Z.
Insurance
2.30     MSEB follows the standard practice of insuring works under
construction and equipment procured for ongoing projects against total or
partial loss. For permanent installations MSEB holds policies which
conform to the accepted utility practice in India in terms of type and
amount of coverage. Assets which are mortgaged with Life Insurance
Corporation are insured, as are assets of hydro power stations which are on
lease from GOMID. The present practice does not provide insurance for
industrial risks and accidental damage to industrial installation.
However, generation related risks do exist, as demonstrated by a recent
accidental fire in a coal handling facility, for which no insurance
coverage was provided for. Under the Action Plan for Managerial
Development (Annex 2.3), MSEB will carry out a comprehensive industrial
risks survey for its operations and contract adequate insurance coverage
(Draft terms of reference for the survey are given in Annex 2.3,
Attachment 8).
III. THE PROJECT
Project Setting
3.01     Because of the high thermal share, peak demand in the Western
Region is being partially met with expensive-to-run thermal plant.
Moreover, the need for thermal plant to operate at partial load during off-
peak hours is shortening considerably equipment life. To correct this
imbalance, the long term expansion plan for the Western Region includes the
installation of a number of peaking plants in the Region. The concept of
the proposed scheme is to expand further the capacity of the existing Koyna
hydropower, capacity allowing the entire installation to operate in a more
peaking mode than at present. This solution, in addition to being the
least cost, minimizes displacement of population and adverse environmental



-26-
effects because no new reservoir is needed and all necessary infrastructure
is in place. Koyna IV vould add 1,000 MW of peaking capacity to the
existing system but only a small quantity of additional energy would be
generated by the Koyna complex, that resulting from the higher efficiency
of the new units relative to the existing ones. The objective of the
project will increase capacity to generate at peak hours at the expense of
generation at times when spare capacity exists elsewhere; shifting Koyna to
peak load duty would result .4n existing thermal units operating at a higher
load factor and increasing their energy generation by about 1390 GWh.
3.02     Interstate power trading in the Western Region is still very
imperfect and does not take full advantage of the large potential economic
gains from integrated operation. The WREB, established to organize power
pooling and dispatching in the Region, has not fully achieved its
objectives because of institutional, technical and operational limitations.
Presently most interstate power transactions are arranged bilaterally by
the SEBs concerned, without full access to information on the best options
available to SEBs at a particular time. Prices are set in an ad-hoc way
with no clear relation to overall economy and efficiency of the system.
While WREB local dispatching is equipped with sophisticated hardware and
software, financed through a previous Bank credit (Credit 377-IN) for
monitoring plant operation and programming plant dispatch, there is still
considerable scope for improvements in interstate power trading.
Understanding was reached during negotiations that GOI and the Bank will
jointly undertake, in parallel with the implementation of the proposed
project, the studies described in Annex 3.10 which will identify
opportunities to improve coordination of regional load dispatch and to
rationalize the commercial arrangements governing interstate power trading.
3.03     The existing Koyna gravity dam was completed in 1961.  In 1967, a
severe earthquake damaged the structure which was repaired and strengthened
with the advice of international experts. Since then, the dam has operated
satisfactorily with no signs of distress or abnormal behavior. HSEB and
GOMID have set up a detailed surveillance system including necessary
instrumentation, periodic inspection of the dam and monitoring of
performance by a panel of experts. Over the years, leakage through the dam
has increased substantially although not yet exceeding normal levels for
this type of dam. Considering the additional investments to be incurred in
Koyna IV and the need to ensure that the life of the dam is preserved for
as long as Koyna IV would be in operation, it is prudent to proceed at this
stage with a thorough inspection of dam condition using modern technology
'i.e. underground cameras, radioisotop tracers, etc.) to: (i) carry out
repairs as necessary to control leakage and any other repairs deemed
necessary after detailed inspection; (ii) upgrade surveillance
instrumentation and monitoring procedures; and (iii) update emergency plans
and procedures. The proposed project includes a component to carry out
these tasks.
3.04     MSEB's five-year system expansion plan for 1989/90-1994/95
includes generation additions (about 2,200 MW), reinforcement and
improvements to the high voltage transmission system (about 3,300 km of
double and single circuit lines with voltages between 110 kV and 400 kV),
as well as improvementi and development of the distribution grids
throughout Maharashtrs. Annex 3.1 (Section B) gives details of the plan.
The Plan is well balanced with emphasis on transmission and distribution,



-27-
two areas which have been lagging behind, impeding a better use of existing
generating capacity and constraining the reduction of system losses which
are presently at 14Z. Generation projects included in the Plan are part of
the least-cost expansion program for the Western Region prepared by CEA.
The configuration of the high-voltage transmission system is also least-
cost and has been determined on the basis of expected demand growth at the
various load centers throughout the State, together with requirements for
power evacuation from proposed and existing generating stations. The
proposed transmission program is consistent with the long term transmission
plan for the Region up to the year 2005 prepared by CEA. Distribution
investments included in MSEB's investment Plan are part of a longer term
(10 year) program to improve and expand the distribution systems in ten
major urban centers throughout the State. The first group of cities in the
Plan includes Thane, Dombivali, Ulhasnagar, Nagpur and Pune. Transmission
and distribution schemes scheduled for implementation during the initial
three years of the Plan, for which Bank financing is proposed under this
Project, have been defined at feasibility level whereas those in the later
years of the plan are still at prefeasibility stage and may undergo changes
in cost and scope as engineering work advances. For this reason the
proposed Bank financing covers only the initial three years of the Plan.
The Plan, when implemented, would result in a more efficient use of
installed plant, better service quality, lower system losses and increased
revenues for MSEB.
Project Objectives
3.05     The Project objective is to improve power supply and operating
efficiency In MSEB and in the Western Region power systems through:
(a) expanding regional peaking capacity at least cost; (b) improving
utilization of existing generating capacity; lc) strengthening and
upgrading MSEB's transmission and distribution systems; (d) recommeniding
ways to improve load dispatch in the Western Region and rationalize bulk
power regional trading: (e) improving MSEB's retail power tariff structure;
(f) strengthening MS!B's finances; and (g) introducing institutional
improvements to MSEB.
Project Description
3.06     The project would consist of:
(a) construction of the fourth stage of the Koyna hydroelectric power
plant comprising:
Ci)    intake structure in the existing Royna reservoir;
(ii)   a 4.5 km long headrace tunnel for 230 m3/s maximum
capacity ending in a 22.8 m diameter, 110 m high surge
tank;
(iii) four steel-lined high pressure shafts 3.9 rn diameter
and 530 m long each;



-28-
(iv)   a 1,000 MW (4x250 MW) underground power station
including a machine hall 22 m wide, 143 m long and
48 m high and a transformers hall 158 m long, 22 m
wide and 18 m high;
(v)    a 2,150 m long tailrace tunnel;
(vi)   a 2,000 m long tunnel for access to the power plant;
and
(vii)  the associated 400 kV switchyard and transmission
lines;
(b)  detailed inspection of the existing Koyna dam, repairs as needed,
upgrading of instrumentation, and review of emergency plans as
required;
(c)  supply and erection of equipment and materials for MSEB five-year
transmission and distribution investment program to be procured
between 1989/90 and 1991/92, and strengthening of MSEB capabilities
for planning of distribution systems;
sd)  implementation of plans to strengthen MSEB's finances, management,
and operations including: (i) financial performance targets and
corresponding actions; (ii) setting up of corporate planning,
opexational performance monitoring, and management accounting
systems; (iii) streamlining internal procedures; and (iv)
accelerating development of data processing and internal audit
systems; and
Ce)  consultancy, training services, including services of POE, and
computer hardware and software required to implement parts (a) to
(e) of the project.
Annex 3.1 gives a detailed description of the project.
Project Engineering
3.07     Koyna IV Power Station.  GOMID has been responsible for the
engineering of the Koyna complex. Engineering work stated in the late 50's
for Stages I and II, which were commissioned between May 1962 and June,
19M7. Stage III was commissioned in 1976. GOMID has been working since
1978 on the engineering of Stage IV, for which designs, draft bidding
documents, and technical specifications for all major contracts have been
completed. GOMID has appointed a five-member Panel of Experts (POE) to
review the technical aspects of the power plant. Annex 3.7 shows the TOR
for the POE. The present composition of the POE may need to be modified to
include a wider range of specialists in fields such as rock mechanics,
geotechnical engineering, dam safety and electromechanical equipment, etc.
and to ensure independent technical review. GOMID agreed to retain the POE
to review the technical aspects of Koyna IV in accordance with the TOR
agreed with the Bank, and to furnish for Bank review the revised composition
of the POE including the qualifications of the proposed members
(para 6.01tb)). The terms of reference for the POE enable it to retain



-29-
temporary members or advisors in specific disciplines. To expedite the
recruitment of temporary or permanent members of the POE, GOHID will
maintain a roster of specialists in different disciplines during the
construction period, from which POE members will be selected. The roster
and the qualifications of included candidates will be furnished to the Bank
for comments no later than December 31, 1989 (para 6.01(a)). Any subsequent
additions to the roster should be acceptable to the Bank. The POE will
oversee the technical aspects of project implementation until commissioning
of the last generating unit.
3.08     GOMID has a cadre of experienced engineers in design and
construction of hydropower stations which will be responsible for the
detailed design and construction supervision of the power station. However,
since completion of the previous stages, underground design and construction
technology have evolved rapidly resulting in more efficient and less
expensive practices. Moreover, the size of the units proposed for Koyna IV
(250 MW, Francis type) is unprecedented in India and no substantial
expe::ience exists in the country with units of similar characteristics.
GOMID will therefore need assistance from consultants with suitable
expertise in design, manufacturing and erection and testing of large
Francis-type generating units. GOIMD will also benefit from assistance of
consultants with expertise in modern construction of underground works.
Moreover, the detailed inspection and subsequent repairs, if necessary, of
the Koyna dam will need specialized assistance in modern dam inspection and
repair technology. GOM has agreed to employ consultants by December 31,
1989, to assist GOMID during the preparation of final engineering,
preparation of technical specifications, inspection of manufacturing of the
generating units and erection and testing of the units. In addition, GOMID
has agreed to appoint, before December 31, 1989, an independent
international dam review panel (DRP) of specialists to: (i) review the
present condition of the Koyna dam; (ii) determine the scope of any
additional investigations that may be required; (iii) establish the need for
specialized consultants for the additional investigations; (iv) approve the
terms of reference for any consultants that may be needed; (v) review the
results of the investigations and the proposed repairs plan; and (vi) review
implementation of the repairs :para 6.01(b)). GOMID will furnish the Bank,
before December 31, 1990, the report of the panel for comment.  Any
consultants determined by the DRP will be appointed before June 30, 1991
(para 6.01(c)). Annex 3.8 shows the suggested terms of reference for the
engineering consultants. About 80 staff-months of consulting services will
be required for this purpose. Annex 3.9 shows the suggested scope of the
investigations and engineering work to be carried out in respect of the
existing Koyna dam.
3.09     Transmission and Distribution Component. MSEB will be responsible
for the engineering of the transmission and distribution components of the
projec;, including the transmission lines to evacuate power from Koyna IV.
Engineering for transmission and distribution schemes to be financed under
the Project are at feasibility level. MSEB has prepared general conditions
for bids, special conditions, conditions of contract and draft technical
specifications for the bulk of materials and equipment to be procured under
the Project. MSEB has sufficient competent engineers and an adequate
organization to undertake the detailed engineering work associated with
these components. However, MSEB's needs for expansion and reinforcement of
distribution systems are large and growing fast. Presently distribution



-30-
planning and optimization of this work, which involves considerable
computational effort, is done manually. MSEB's planning and design
capabilities need to be enhanced by the provision of suitable computerized
design aids that will permit system planning and design at a rate
commensurate with its needs, and the preparation of more economical
development distribution plans, and the refining of the schemes proposed
under this component. Moreover, MSEB is not fully exploiting existing
design options whJich could substantially reduce costs of system improvements
and development. The proposed project provides for assistance to HSEB to
improve distribution planning and to enhance MSEB's capabilities in this
regard. MSEB will, as part of the proposed Action Plan for Managerial
Development (Annex 2.3): (i) acquire and install hardware and software
necessary to implement computerized planning and design for distribution
systems; and (ii) implement a training program for distribution staff
including, inter-alia, operation of computerized planning and design and
familiarization with current trends and practices in distribution planning
and design elsewhere in the world. Attachment 1 to Annex 2.3 gives the
proposed scope of the program. About 20 staff-months of coneultancy
services will be required to net up the computerlzed design system and train
MSEB's staff in its use.
Project Implementation and Construction Schedule
3.10     Koyna IV Power Station.  The project will be completed over a
period of six-and-a-half years starting mid-19&9. GOMID will be responsible
for implementation of the Koyna IV power station. The first unit of Koyna
IV will be commissioned in December 1994 and the fourth unit in December
1995. Being an extension of an existing power plant, much of the necessary
infrastructure such as housing, access roads, communications facilities,
etc. are already in place. Road access to all working fronts of the plant
has been completed except for the 1.5 km road to the top of the surge tank
which will be part of the civil works contract for the headrace tunnel.
GOMID is presently excavating several access tunnels and the tailrace tunnel
on force account and using local contractors. These works which are not
financed by the Bank will be handed over to the main civil contractors
allowing rapid access to excavation of the headrace tunnel and power house
which are critical. GOMID has established a project unit to implement this
component. Annex 3.14 shows the organization proposed by GOMID which is
generally satisfactory. The Bank has proposed modifications to the
crganization to strengthen specific areas which presently appear inadequate
for the proposed tasks. GOMID will revise the organization of the project
unit as agreed with the Bank. GOMID agreed to provide, before December 31,
1989, a staffing plan and description of qualifications and experience
required for all positions at superintending engineer level and above.
Furthermore, GOMID will give the Bank an opportunity to comment on any
substantial changes to the agreed organization before they are implemented
(para ;}O.01(d)).
3.11     Transmission and Distribution Component.  MSEB will implement the
transmission and distribution components of the Project. Procurement of
materials and equipment for transmission and distribution will be carried
out between 1989 and 1992. Erection works will take place in parallel but
may extend until 1994/95 as part of the 1989190 - 1994/95 Investment Plan of
MSEB. No special organization is required for implementation of these



K
-31-
components because MSEB is adequately organized to carry out the works
envisaged under the project as part of its normal operations. In order to
facilitate project monitoring and control of Bank financed investments, MSEB
agreed to prepare and furnish to the Bank, no later than March 1st of every
year, a work plan including the list of transmission lines and substations
to be financed by the Bank in the following financial year, the list of
contract packages for which tenders will be invited, and the associated
timetable for procurement of equipment and materials to be financed by the
Bank (para 6.02(c)). Considering that several MSEB units will be involved
in the implementation of the project, MSEB agreed during negotiations to
establish, before December 31, 1989 a Project Cell under the Member
(Technical) to: (a) monitor project implementation in a centralized way; (b)
serve as liaison with GOI, CEA, other government agencies and the Bank on
matters related to Bank financed schemes; (c) prepare periodic project
progress reports for concerned authorities and for the Bank; and (d)
coordinate preparation of application for disbursement of Bank funds. The
Cell wili eventually be transferred to the Planning Unit to be created in
MSEB (Annex 2.3).
Water Rights
3.12     There are no pending water rights issues that could affect the
operation of the proposed Koyna IV power station. The scheme is located on
the Koyna river which is a tributary of the Krishna river. The waters of
the Krishna river are shared by Maharashtra, Andhra Pradesh, and Karnataka.
The Krishna Waters Tribunal was set to allocate water to different uses and
shares to the riparian states. The diversion of water for power generation
from the Koyna river, will be in compliance with Krishna Waters Tribunal
Award. The Tribunal determined that as from June, 1994, nc more than
1,911 million m3 per year could be diverted for power garep:at'on at Koyna.
The Tribunal decision has been taken into account in the planning of the
scheme.
Resettlement and Land Acquisition
3.13     Koyna IV Power Station.  Resettlement for the first three stages of
this scheme was completed in 1960 to 1970. When the Maharashtra Project
Displaced Persons Act of 1976 was passed, the provisions of this Act were
extended to the families displacetd by the earlier stages of the Koyna
Project. The provisions of the Act are consistent with the Bank's
guidelines for resettlement and rehabilitation. Construction of the Koyna
IV power plant requires resettlement of unly six landless families in
Ratnagiri district. One of these families has already moved voluntarily and
the others have agreed to shift to lands provided by the Revenue Department.
3.14     The construction of Koyna Stage IV requires 479 hectares of private
land belonging to farming families residing in neighboring villages. Land
is required for spoil areas, quarries, temporarv storage and afforestation
programs. Of the total land required, some 118 hectares, which includes all
critical areas necessary to start construction, have been acquired and
another 345 hectares are expected to be in possession of GOMID before June
1989. The remaining 16 hectares have been requested from the District
Collector, Ratnagiri for processing. While no houses (village sites) will
be affected, the families owning this land could be losing income from their



-32-
productive farmlands and will be therefore entitled to resettlement and
rehabilitation under the provisions of the Maharashtra Resettlement of
Project Displaced Persons Act of 1976. Before December 31, 1989, GOMID will
furnish a report to th, Bank on the individual cases of both landowning and
landless families affected including applicability of the Act. GOMID will
subsequently, on the basis of the report, compensate the families in
accordance with the Act (para 6.01(e)).
3.15     Transmission and Distribution Component.  MSEB requires about
250 hectares of land for construction of all the substations contemplated in
the 1989/90 - 1994/95 investment plan. MSEB surveys proposed sites for new
substations on a rolling basis throughout the year, in accordance with its
needs for commissioning of new facilities. After surveys are completed,
MSEB proceeds to acquire the land and to compensate any persons displaced.
Presently the proportions of these lands owned by government and by private
individuals is unknown, but in most cases the land is occupied and
resettlement and rehabilitation of landowners, renters and squatters will be
required. At present, the exact number of persons to be displaced and the
amount of compensation to be paid are not known, but provision has been made
in the cost estimate of the sub-stations based on MSEB's recent experience.
Cost variations in this respect are not expected to exceed 1 or 2? of the
total project cost. MSEB's past compensation practice did not conform with
the Maharashtra Resettlement of Project Displaced Persons Act of 1976.  MSEB
will resettle and rehabilitate any persons displaced by MSEB's schemes in
accordance with the provisions of the 1976 Act as it may be amended from
time to time. MSEB agreed to prepare and furnish to the Bank, no later than
March 1st of every year, as part of the annual work plan mentioned in para
3.11, the resettlement and rehabilitation plan for persons displaced by any
substations under the project. The resettlement plan for persons displaced
should be based on actual surveys of the land to be acquired for the
substations. The first plan is expected to be furnished to the Bank on
December 31, 1989 (para 6.02(c)); Annex 3.11 shows the details to be
included in the plan. No resettlement of population is required for
transmission lines because only right-of-way is necessary. Landowners are
presently compensated for any loss of revenue stemming from right-of-way
restrictions or for any damage, normally minor, caused by construction of
the lines. Present arrangements in this respect are satisfactory.
Environmental Aspects
3.16     Koyna IV Power Station.  The environmental impact of Koyna IV is
negligible because no new reservoir is required and works are underground.
The Department of Environment of GOI has cleared the Koyna IV scheme. GOM
needs to obtain from the Department of Forest (DOF) the forest clearance for
about 2 hectares required for access road to the surge tank of the power
station. If an access road is not cleared, GOMID has devised an alternative
completely underground solution, which does not require forest clearance.
GOMID has applied for forest clearance for the road which should be obtained
no later than December 31, 1989 in order not to delay construction the power
plant. If clearance is not granted before December 31, 1989, GOMID will
consider alternative access (para 6.01(f)) or adoption of the underground
solution. Moreover, in order to minimize any adverse effect of construction
operations on the environmjnt, understanding was reached that GOMID will



-33-
restore all construction affected areas to an environmentally sound
coadition by adequate afforestation and treatment.  This requirement will be
part of the civil works contracts which will include specifications for
environmental treatment and corresponding pay items.
3.17     Transmission and Distribution Component.  MSEB plans to build
during 1989-92 some 1,500 to 1,800 km of transmission lines with voltages
between 132 kV to 400 kV. These lines will need about 7,000 hectares of
land for right-of-way for which MSEB will require to obtain clearance of the
Ministry of Revenue and Forests, GOM, and of the Ministry of Environment and
Forest, GOI. Procedures involved are time consuming and lengthy. MSEB
staff are well informed and able to utilize these procedures effectively as
there is a rolling program for obtaining forest clearances as part of the
normal system expansion in Maharashtra. MSEB will furnish, as part of the
annual work plan mentioned in para 3.11, evidence that forest clearance has
been obtained for all transmission lines proposed for Bank financing during
that year.
3.18     Environmental affairs are becoming an issue of major concern.
There is increased sensitivity and public interest in environmental
protection on account of the accelerated ecological degradation taking place
in the country. MSEB has to deal with this issue on a number of fronts, for
example pollution control (air, water, land) in design and operation of
thermal power plants, forest protection, water resources management for
hydropower schemes, monitoring of compliance with environmental standards
for construction and operation of power schemes, processing of clearances in
a timely way, etc.  These activities are presently dispersed throughout
different units in the SEB and there is no systematic and coordinated
approach to environmental issues for the SEB as a whole. Under the Action
Plan for Managerial Development (Annex 2.3, para 2.5), MSEB will establish,
by June 30, 1990, an Environmental and Forest Affairs Unit directly under
the Board Member (Technical) to coordinate and monitor environmental and
resettlement work in MSEB and provide training to the staff of this new
unit. Attachment 9 to Annex 2.3 shows the proposed terms of reference for
the new unit.

-35-
The total cost of the Project including physical and price contingencies
(but excluding about US$70 million equivalent of taxes and duties) is about
$1,008 million equivalent of which US$540 million (541) represents the
direct and indirect foreign exchange costs. Interest during construction
add another US$265 million to the financing required. The project costs are
based on January 1989 forecast prices which were derived from: (a)
contractor-type analysis of unit prices (heavy-construction estimating
method) prepared for the civil works of Koyna IV; (b) recent quotations
received for similar equipment and miscellaneous civil works in India; and
(c) inquiries with manufacturers of major rotating equipment carried out in
1987. Prices have been updated to April 1989 on the basis of estimated
inflation for 1988189. Quantities were obtained from designs at tender
level for the power plant and from feasibility estimates for the
transmission and distribution components. The cost of consulting services
was based on an estimated 160 staff-months, of which about 40 staff-months
are expected to be from local consultants. In addition to the consulting
services, an allowance of 9.52 of the base cost has been made for
engineering and administration of the power plant, and of 52 for other
Project components. Physical contingencies have been provided for as
follows: (a) 252 of the base cost for all underground works; (b) 102 of the
base cost for all other works; (c) 101 of the base cost for materials and
equipment. These allowances are in line with the status of Project design
and with the risks of extra costs for underground works such as those
contemplated under the proposed Project. Price contingencies, which amount
to 212 of the base cost, are based on expected annual inflation rates of 92
for 1988/89, 82 for 1989/90, 72 for 1990/91-93 and 6.52 per year thereafter
for local costs and 5.32 for 1988189-90, 52 for 1990/91 and 4.12 per year
thereafter for foreign costs over the entire construction period. Interest
during construction were calculated on the MSEB component and on the GOM
component separately based on their respective implementation periods and on
their individual local and foreign costs. It has been assumed that US$ 220
million of the power station investment cost will be ftraded on Bank terms,
and that 202 of the MSEB transmission and distribution component will be
funded by equity and 802 by loan from GOI bearing an interest of 112 per
annum, which is the current average interest rate for MSEB's long term
borrowing.
Project Financing
3.20     The proposed Bank financing for this Project is US$400 million,
equivalent to about 312 of the total financing requirements, net of duties
and taxes. In order to finance the foreign exchange not financed by the
Bank, GOI will seek cofinancing for the project, particularly for major
equipment, from bilateral donors, export and suppliers' credits or
commercial banks. There is scope for cofinancing of materials and equipment
for the transmission and distribution component estimated at about US$150
million. GOI has already received expressions of interest frcm a number of
donor countries and suppliers of equipment. In case cofinancing
materializes for any item presently envisaged to be financed by the Bank,
the Bank will reallocate any Bank funds no longer needed for that item to
other elements of the project. Any sums not financed by cofinanciers will
be financed by GOI, GOM and, for the MSEB component, MSEB's own generated
funds. GOI will bear the exchange rate and interest rate risks. The Bank
will not finance IDC. Table 3.2 gives the project financing plan.

3.23     All civil works contracts for MSEB's transmission and distribution
schemes financed by the Bank with an estimated cost of US$5 million
equivalent or more will be subject to ICB. All Bank financed contracts for
supply of goods exceeding US$200,000 will also 3.6 subject to ICO, except
Bank financed survey equipment, laboratory instruments and equipment,
specialized office engineering equipment and computer hardware and software
up to an aggregate cost of US$5 million which may be procured through
international or local shopping procedures satisfactory to the Bank. Other
materials, equipment, and civil works will be procured under LCB
procedures. Foreign suppliers will not be precluded from participating in
LCB. The Bank is satisfied that LCB procedures used by MSEB will result in
efficient and economic procurement of those elements not financed by the
Bank for the transmission and distribution program. Procurement of the
bulk of Bank financed goods for MSEB transmission and distribution program
will be carried out in three major rounds, one every year, in 1989/90,
1990191 and 1991192. To take advantage of low prices resulting from bulk
supplies, contracts may be awarded for phased deliveries over a period
extending beyond a one year horizon. 'Zontractors for construction of the
headrace tunnel and for the civil works of the Koyna IV power house and
suppliers of the turbo-generating units for Koyna IV will be prequalified.
Except for the access and tailrace tunnels to the Koyna IV power plant
(para 3.11), no other major works will be undertaken by force account.
Local civil contractors, competing under ICB, will have a 7.52 of
preference margin and local manufacturers a 152 preference or the
applicable duty, whichever is less. Major local firms are expected to be
competitive in civil works and in most of the equipment supply tenders.
Consultants will be employed in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for
employment of consultants by the Bank and its borrowers. All Bank financed
civil works contracts, with an estimated cost of US$5 million equivalent or
more will be subject to the Bank's prior review. All Bank financed
contracts for supply of goods, with an estimated cost of US$1 million
equivalent or more, will also be subject to Bank prior review. Other Bank
financed contracts will be subject to selective post award review. About
10 contract packages for civil works and 35 contract packages for equipment
will be subject to prior Bank review, representing 90Z of the total cost of
Bank financed items. In order to simplify Bank review of documents, MSEB
and GOMID have agreed to use standard bidding documents for all Bank
financed contracts.
Disbursemuents
3.24     Disbursements of Bank funds will be made against: (a) 1002 of the
CIF cost or of the ex-factory cost (if manufactured in India) of equipment
and materials procured under ICB and international shopping for Koyna IV,
repairs of Koyna dam, MSEB transmission and distribution schemes included
in the annual programs mentioned in para 3.11; (b) 502 of locally procured
equipment for the same project components mentioned in (a); (c) 702 of the
cost cf civil works for Koyna IV including the supply and erection of the
penstocks and the repairs to the dam up to a maximum of US$40 million



-38-
equivalent and 152 thereafter; (d) 60Z of the cost of civil works and
erection of lines and substations for MSEB's transmission and distribution
schemes included in the annual programs mentioned in para 3.12; and (e)
10OZ of consulting and training services, including POE's and DRP's
services. Materials and goods for MSEB's transmission and distribution
component (except the lines for Koyna IV) will be eligible for Bank
financing only if contracts are awarded before December 31, 1992 (para
6.02(d)). Disbursements will be fully documented except for payments
against contracts for training, equipment, materials, consultants, and
compensatory afforestation contracts each less than US$200,000 equivalent,
and against contracts for civil works each less than US$500,000.  Such
disbursements will be made against statements of expenditures (SOEs), the
documentation of which will not be sent to the Bank but retained by GOMID,
MSEB and CEA, as appropriate, for inspection by the supervision missions.
Standard procedures for auditing SOEs will apply. To facilitate
disbursements, a special account will be established by GOI with an
authorized allocation of US$20 million. Annex 3.6 shows a comparison of
the project disbursement profile and the standard profile for power
projects in India. In the initial four years of project implementation,
the project cumulative profile is about 52 higher th&n the standard profile
because of the time-slice approach used for the transmission and
distribution component. Annex 3.6 also shows the schedule of estimated
disbursements for Bank funds. The closing date for the loan will be
December 31, 1996.
Project Operation
3.25     The Koyna IV power plant will belong to GOM but be leased for 30
years to MSEB, which will be fully responsible for its operation and
maintenance. With the addition of 1,000 MW, the Koyna complex will operate
as a peaking facility for about 4 hours a day throughout the year. The
average annual plant factor will be 17Z. (The present installation
operates as a mid-range facility with a load factor of about 37Z). The
power plant will add 870 MW of effective capacity to the system (after
allowing for scheduled and forced outages). Since there is no addition of
water to the Koyna complex, the installation will add only capacity to the
system and a small amount of firm energy (as a result of its more efficient
units). The transmission system to link the Koyna IV power plant to the
Western Region grid is part of the proposed transmission component of this
project. The power evacuation system comprises one 400 kV double circuit
line from Koyna to Karad substation (80 km) and another 400 kV Koyna to
Lonikand double circuit line (180 km) and extension of the existing
substations at the Karad and Lonikand. GOM will furnish to the Bank, no
later than December 31, 1989, a satisfactory timetable of key actions for
implementation of the transmission lines to connect Koyna IV to the Western
Grid. GOM will implement the project in accordance with the agreed
timetable (para 6.01(g)).
Project Monitoring
3.26     GOMID and MSEB will furnish to the Bank quarterly progress reports
covering physical works, consultancy services, costs, disbursements and
administrative aspects of the project. MSEB will be responsible for
coordinating the preparation of the quarterly report covering all project



-39-
components. In addition, MSEB vill furnish to the Bank annual reports of
its financial and operational results and of the Board's administrative and
managerial situation. The first quarterly progress report will be made
available to the Bank ro later than March 31, 1990.
Project Risks
3.27     The proposed Koyna IV scheme presents the usual risks associated
with major underground works, such as unexpected geological conditions that
may adversely affect project cost or the construction schedule (or both).
However, the geological exploration program carried out thus far and the
excavation works carried out for the neighboring Stages I to III, which are
also underground, indicate that the geological setting is adequate for the
proposed work, and that risks are acceptably low. The size of the turbo-
generating units proposed for Koyna IV (250 MW Francis type) is
unprecedented in India and GOMID does not thus have the necessary expertise
to deal with the selection, specifications, and inspection of manufacturing
of the turbines. To minimize the risk of technical problems, GOKID will
hire for assistance consultants specialized in this field. The
transmission and distribution components do not pose any extraordinary
technical risks since there is ample knowledge in India and in MSEB of the
technologies involved. Implementation of MSEB's proposed five-year
investment plan may be affected by delays in administrative approvals,
forese clearances and land acquisition. Budgetary constraints may also
require rescheduling of investments included in the plan. To deal with
these risks, adequate allowances for administrative processing have been
incorporated in the implementation schedule. Moreover, MSEB agreed
(para 4.16' to review with the Bank every year the updated investment
program for the following five years. As part of the review, the Bank will
need to be satisfied as to the nature and scope of any revisions needed and
the integrity of the updated program in terms of its internal consistency,
technical adequacy, and economic justification. It is possible that as a
result of rescheduling of investments, MSEB may be unable to commit the
entire loan amount before December 31, 1992 (para 3.26). To keep this risk
at a low level, the Bank envisages financing only 252 of MSEB planned
investment during the initial three years of the plan. This will give
ample flexibility to MSEB for selecting those schemes for Bank financing
which are not ccnrtrained by issues of the nature described above. There
is a risk that GOM may not allow MSEB to implement tariff adjustments
necessary to achieve the targetted 4? rate of return by FY 1993/94. Annual
tariff adjustment 1? below the targetted 7.8? annual average involves a 32
lower ROR, and a financial gap of Rs 10 billions over five years, to be
filled by GOM. Tariff adjustment 12 below inflation would reduce MSEB's
rate of return to zero, and require a contribution from GOM of Rs 70
billions over the Eighth Plan period. Although the State government
considers tariff increases with some reluctance, its past record in this
respect is impressive, with a 5? per annum real increase over the last
three years, and the budgetary implication of insufficient tariff
adjustments makes tariff adjustments below inflation rather unlikely.



-40-
IV. FINANCE
A. Introduction
4.01     Maharashtra State Electricity Board is the largest SEB in India,
with revenue in FY 1987/88 of Rs 20 billions (US$1.3 billions),
representing about 182 of total sector revenue for all-India. It manages
and operates fixed assets estimated at Rs 50 billions (increasing by about
Rs 5 billions every year) with a higher than averaae efficiency
(Annex 2.2). Although the overall financial perfcsftance of SEBs in India
is rather weak (para 1.17), MSEB stands out as a notable exception: its net
income has been consistently positive over the past five years; it met the
3Z after interest Rate of Return target established by GOI; and it has
claimed Rural Electrification Subsidies from the GOM only in exceptional
years. However, MSEB's finances suffer from an imbalanced capital
structure with an insufficient equity basis (it has a 92/08 debt/equity
ratio), resulting in high debt service. Moreover, working capital
management has been less than sa_isfactory: commercial receivables (mainly
from other States) increased recently and exceeded 3 months of revenues in
FY 1987/88 but have been reduced to 2.6 months as of January 31, 1989. In
future, rapidly increasing demand (para 1.11) will necessitate capital
investment of Rs 8 to 20 billions annually, to be funded in an increasing
proportion (up to 21Z) from self-generated funds, in order to limit
recourse to GOM funds. MSEB's creditworthiness will be strengthened with a
debt coverage of at least 1.2, and its equity base will be strengthened
from retained earaings, so as to achieve a 83/17 debt/equity ratio by
FY 1994/95. Also, commercial receivables will be reduced to 2.5 months of
revenues by FY 1989/90. Agreement was reached with MSEB during
negotiations on the content and implementation of a Financial Action Plan
(Annex 4.4) to strengthen the Board's longer term financial position (para
6.02(e)). The main actions included in the Financial Action Plan are
discussed in detail in the ensuing paragraphs.
4.02     For the preparation of the proposed project, the Bank and the
financial department of MSEB carried out a detailed diagnosis of the
financial position and perspectives for the Board. The main conclusions of
the diagnosis are that:
(i)   MSEB has a reasonably satisfactory financial performance in
terms of profitability with a 3Z rate of return on net fixed
assets at historical value in FY 1987/88 (9.6Z Bank ROR
equivalent on historical net fixed assets);
(ii) MSEB's liquidity position has gradually deteriorated as a
result of underfunding of investment as well as increasing
level cf receivables due particularly by neighbouring
States. The cash shortage issue has been addressed by
increasing short-term payables, but more particularly by
changes in FY 1986/87 in the terms of GOM loans, which
include now a six-year deferral of interest and principal
payments; and
(iii) in the absence of sufficient funding of capital investment
from equity, MSEB has become heavily indebted.



-41-
4.03      Remedies to identified weaknesses have been discussed with MSEB's
management and GOX. They include: (a) increasing profitability through
combined tariff adjustments and cost reduction, to achieve and maintain an
acceptable debt service coverage and strengthen MSEB's equity position from
its self-generated resources; (b) maintaining future growth of debt
compared to equity within reasonable limit,; (c) ensuring that GO  loans
for capital investment correspond to a more realistic estimate of MSEB's
expansion program, by improving coordination between investment funding and
plans: and (d) pursuing efforts to reduce working capital requirement by
reducing commercial receivables to their pre-1983/84 level of 2.5 months of
revenues. Financial projections have been developed on that basis,
indicating that the financial position of MSEB would then significantly
improve by 1994195.

and 1987/88 are shown in Table 4.1. Annexes 4.1 to 4.3 present MSEB's

4.05      Profitability.  Since 1983, MSEB has maintained a reasonably
satisfactory financial position in terms of net income and contribution to
capital investment. Profitability since 1984/85 has been steady with a net
income of Re 500 to 700 million; pre-1984185 performance is not strictly
comparable, because of the change in accounting practices under the new
accounting system, and non-capitalization of interest during construction
(IDC). However, there has been some deterioration in gross cash generated
from operations from 24? of revenues in FY 1982/83 to 212 in FY 1987/88,
because of the rapid increase in personnel cost, prior to the 1985/86 freeze
on recruitment, and increasing expensive power purchases. In terms of
contribution to capital investment after debt service payment and working
capital financing, MSEB financed from 9.5Z to 19Z of its capital investment
program; however, this ostensibly satisfactory performance was attributable
to MSEB low debt service prior to FY 1985/86 (prior to FY 1985/86, GOM loans
were not repayable), and to the moratorium on interest and principal on new
GOM loans since FY 1986/87 (para 4.14).
4.06      Liquidity Position.  MSEB's liquidity position over the FY 1982/83
to 1987/88 period has deteriorated. In the past, MSEB's long-term financial
resources (own funds, long-term loans and consumers contribution) were
barely sufficient to cover capital investment (Table 4.2). Moreover,
commercial receivables have increased by Rs 1.5 to 2 billion every year
since FY 1985/86 and have reached 3.7 montbs of sales, resulting in
increasing liquidity difficulties, which were financed through increasing
short-term payables. including payables to suppliers, and higher advances of
consumers for future consumption. In the future, the increasing trend in
short-term payables and receivables is expected to be reversed, and MSEB's
working capital will be strengthened (paras 4.17 and 4.18).
