4.07     Indebtedness.  The capital structure of HSEB displays a permanent
weakness in the small proportion of equity as comDarea to long-term debt.
The Board Debt/Equity ratio w&s from 89/11 to 92/08 over the FY 1982/83 to
nY 1987/88 period, with little improvement, as HSEB's annual retained
earnings were negligible, and GOM did not contribute to HSEB's equity. The
high level of indebtedness of the Board is a major factor contributing to
the current liquidity constraint, which has forced MSEB to resort to
exceptional financial practices for temporary relief (para 4.17), The
rationale for these exceptional practices was that on the one hand long-
term financing sources available to MSEB provided for loans with maturities
in the range of 6 to 24 years; on the other hand, annual depreciation,
fully reflected in costs of operations, is calculated on a linear basis,
over a period of time close to the asset's economic life, which frequently
exceeds 30 years. With tariffs set at a sufficient level to cover total
costs of operations, including depreciation calculated over more than 30
years, plus a 3Z ROR, funds available after payment of working expenses and
interest are insufficient to cover debt repayment calculated over 6 to 24
years, except under special arrangements such as non-repayable loans (which
became repayable from FY 1985/86 onward), as was the case prior to
FY 1985/86, or loans with deferred interest and principal payments, as is
the case now. In future, MSEB's debt/equity ratio will be reduced to
mai.atain debt service within reasonable limits (para 4.14).
C. Future Financial Situation and Performance Targets
4.08     MSEB faces serious financing issues in the future, in order to
maintain its financial viability and creditworthiness, while at the same
time satisfying demand for energy which is expected to increase from 23,899
GWh in FY 1987/88 to 41,038 GWh by FY 1994/95 (7.7? per annum). Capital
investment necessary to satisfy future demand will be much larger than in
the past: in the 19808 annual capital investment ranged from Rs. 3 to 7
billions, while in future, it will range from Rs 8 to 25 billions annually.
MSED's resource mobilization policy underpinning the Financial Action Plan
(Annex 4.4) aims at balancing the financing burden of i.ts development
between (a) consumers, through reasonable tariff adjustments; (b) GOM,
through Government funding; and (c) MSEB, through increased productivity
It also aims at avoiding any build-up of unmanageable long term debt and
debt servicing. Table 4.3 summarizes expected future financial performance
indicators for MSEB. Pro-forma Income Statements, Balance Sheets, and
Sources and Applications of Funds are given in Annexes 4.1 to 4.3; the main
underlying assumptions are detailed in Annex: 4.5, and detailed supporting
tables for performance indicators, future capital investment, financing
plan, working capital schedule, and transfers from GOM are given in Annexes
4.6 to 4.10.

Profitability and Creditworthiness
4.09      In the future, total costs of sales per kWh are projected to
remain constant in real terms over the FY 1988/89 to FY1994/95 period
(Annex 4.10). Ownership costs (depreciation plus interest charged to
operations) will increase substantially (about 32 per annum in real terms)
dr-' to the increasing cost of commercial borrowing, while working expenses
(f"el, personnel, consumables, services and lease costs) per kWh will
declined by 0.82 per annum in real terms, because of the increasing
productivity of labor. Full coverage of cost of sales plus a contribution
to capital investment of about 21Z will require that average tariff per kWh
be adjusted by about 1.2Z per annum in real terms over the FY 1988/89 - FY
1994/95 period.
4.10      MSEr hsas undertaken an extensive program for modernization of
operations (both in the technical and the administrative sectors) which is
expected to raise personnel productivity. The Manpower Plan to be prepared
by MSEB (para 2.18) will be based on a labour force increase of about 22
per annum as stated in the agreed Financial Action Plan (Annex 4.4), while
sales, and the number of consumers will increase by 8Z and 9.5Z per annum
respectively. The corresponding 52 to 62 productivity increase will



-45-
generate annual savings of about Rs 4 billions. Personnel productivity
increase is reflected in Table 4.4 below, in the decreasing labor cost per
kWh (from 0.124 in FY 1988189 to 0.087 in FY 1994195) and the remaining
number of consumers per employee (from 67 in FY 1987188 to 110 in FY
1994/95).
4.11     Despite the coummissioning of more efficient thermal units plants
(500 MW each), no reduction in fuel cost per kWh sold is projected. This
is due to the anticipated decline in coal quality, not fully reflected in
coal price structure, and the effect of more stringent emission standards
on internal consumption and unit efficiency.

4.12     Ownership costs per kWh sold are projected to increase in real
terms, because of MSEB's substantial generation expansion program, but also
because of its heavy capital investment in transmission (para 4.16 and
Annex 4.6). Interest charges per kWh sold is projected to increase,
because of rising long term debt (Rs 43 billions in FY 1987/88 and
Rs 120 billions in FY 1994/95), despite MSEB financing an increasing share
of its capital investment (para 4.15), and also because of the increasing
cost of borrowed funds: presently, the debt still includes "old" loans on
concessional terms but new loans are contracted at rates ranging from 92
for GOM loans to 14.7Z for IDBI loans.
4.13     In order to offset these increases in cost of sales, to finance
working capital requirements, and to contribute adequately to future
capital investment, electricity tariffs should be raised by an average 8.4Z
per annum in nominal terms over the 1988/95 period (Annex 4.1). Projected
annual tariffs are given in Table 4.3 and detailed in Annex 2.8; the
highest increases should take place after 1992/93 in order to support
MSEB's infrastructure building efforts and higher ROR target. MSEB's
profitability, measured by its cash generation before debt service and its
net income, is projected to improve, with a gross cash generation of Rs 4.1



-46-
billions and a net income of Rs 0.7 billions in PY 1987188, and
corresponding figures of Rs 15.9 billions and Rs 4.2 billions in FY 1994195
(Table 4.3 and Annex 4.1).
4.14     Future cash generation should cover long-term debt service,
working capital requirements, and MSEB's own contribution to capital
investment. Presently, MSEB's low level of debt service coverage (1.05 on
a cash basis equivalent to 1.14 on an accrual basis excluding IDC) does not
allow it to contribute significantly to its capital investment needs from
its own funds. The ongoing practice for GOM loans to bear a six-year
moratorium on interest and principal payments will push debt service
payments below the level of accrued debt service (excluding IDC). However,
deferred payments will come after six years as additional debt service
payable over 24 years period, but not to be charged to operations in these
years. This practice is acceptable provided: (i) tariff level is set based
on requirements to meet debt service accrued, which will be substantially
higher than debt service paid; (ii) the corresponding cash savings are
utilized to increase HSEB's self-financing rate, thereby reducing new
borrowings for capital investment and future debt service when rescheduled
debts become payable; and (iii) financial performance is evaluated on the
basis of debt service accrued, and long-term debt including deferred
interest. Alternative solutions to strengthen MSEB's debt servicing
capacity exist, such as conversion of some of GOM loans into equity, or
perpetual interest bearing loans. Under the Financial Action Plan (Annex
4.4, para 2.3), GOM and MSEB will examine by March 31, 1990, alternative
financial packages and financing terms for GOM contributions to MSEB's
capital investment program. With an improved earning capacity (para 4.13)
and the investment financing policy developed in para 4.16, MSEB's debt
service coverage ratio on accrual basis is projected to improve from 1.14
at present to 1.20 in FY 1989/90, and above 1.20 in FY 1990/91 and
thereafter -- equivalent to 1.05 at present, 1.09 in FY 1989/90, 1.19 in
FY 1990/91, and up to 1.65 in FY 1994/95, on a cash basis (Annex 4.4, para
3). Agreement was reached with MSEB during negotiations that it will take
all necessary measures, including tariff adjustments, so that cash
generated from operations divided by interest accrued, (defined as interest
charged to operations), plus debt repayment, is higher than 1.00 in FY
1989/90, and 1.20 in FY 1990/91 and thereafter (para 6.02(f)).
Indebtedness and Capital Structure
4.15     Presently, MSEB is financed 962 on borrowed funds (para 4.07).
Equity is only Rs 3.7 billions (4Z of long-term funds), of which
Rs 2.4 bllions are consumer contributions, and long-term debt represents
Rs 43.4 billions (comprising Rs 24 billions of GOM loans, and including Rs
6.9 billions of deferred debt service on GOM loans). This high level of
indebtedness, which is one of the causes of MSEB's liquidity constraints,
will be gradually reduced by raising MSEB's own contribution to investment
from its FY 1987/88 level of 0.5Z up to 8Z by FY 1990/91, 112 in
FY 1991/92, 13Z in FY1992/93, and 202 or more from FY 1993/94 onward, as
indicated in the agreed Financial Action Plan (Annex 4.4). Financial
projections indicate that to achieve these capital investment self-
financing rates, and the profitability level mentioned in para 4.13, MSEB's
financial rate of return (after interest charged to operations and
calculated on net fixed assets at historical value) should be no less than
3Z in FY 1989/90 and FY 1990/91, 3.5Z in FY 1991/92 and FY 1992/93, and 42



-47-
from FY 1993/94 onward as agreed with MSEB (para 6.02(g)), (equivalent to
respectively 5.52, 6Z, and 6.31 on revalued net fixed assets, according to
Bank definition). This will permit equity to reach Rs 9.5 billions by FY
1990/91; Rs 12.3 billions in FYl991/92 and Rs 24.6 billions by FY 1994/95,
corresponding to a Debt/ Equity ratio of 87/13; 86/14; and 83/17 in the
corresponding years (Annex 4.4, para 3), as indicated in the agreed
Financial Action Plan.
Capital Investment Plan
4.16     MSEB's capital investment program for the FY 1987/88 to FY 1994/95
period is given in Annex 4.6. It was reviewed and found to be technically
and economically sound. The proposed five-year plan envisages an increase
in capital investment, including IDC, from Rs 7.1 billions in FY 1987/88 up
to Rs 26.8 billions in FY 1994195 (a 20Z annual increase). The priority
given by MSEB to transmission and distribution is reflected in high capital
investment in the sector from FY 1991/92 to FY 1993/94 (about Rs 6 billions
annually); investment in generation will increase from Rs 3 billions in FY
1988/89 to Rs 13 billions in FY 1994/95, to meet increasing demand; and
capital investment in rural electrification is projected to decrease from
Rs 2.6 billions in FY 1988/89 to Rs 1.3 billions in FY 1994/95, as
Maharashtra's rural electrification program approaches completion.  Capital
investment program in generation and in transmission for the outer years of
the plan may need to be adjusted, to take into account limitations in the
MSEB's implementing capacity, possible budgetary constraints (para 4.20),
and possible changes in the demand pattern. As part of MSEB's Corporate
Plan (Annex 2.3), the investment plan will be revised annually. Agreement
was reached with MSEB during negotiations that annual revisions to the
five-year revolving capital investment and financing plan will be discussed
with the Bank, by December 31 of each year (para 6.02(h)).
Working Capital
4.17     Receivables.  Projected working capital requirements of MSEB
depend on its ability to recover expeditiously commercial receivables,
which have steadily increased from 2.5 months of revenues (Rs 1.2 billions)
in FY 1982/83 to 3.7 months in FY 1987/88 (Rs 5.5 billions). The analysis
of commercial receivables by category of consumers indicates that the
situation is markedly different for receivables from neighboring States on
the one hand, and other consumers on the other hand. For the latter
category (about 6.5 millions of consumers), receivables, including
consumption billed but not yet payable, represent on the average an
acceptable 1.5 months of revenues; for neighboring States (Gujarat,
Karnataka, and Goa), receivables increased from 4 months in FY 1982/83 to
8 months in FY 1987/88. However, the most recent information indicates
that following MSEB's efforts to redress the situation, commercial
receivables excluding disputed amounts have been reduced to less than Rs
4.3 billions (2.6 months of sales). Agreement was reached with MSEB during
negotiations that total commercial receivables excluding disputed amounts,
but including consumption billed but not yet payable, will be reduced to
less than 2.5 months of revenues from FY 1989/90 onward (para 6.02(i)), as
agreed under the on-going Chandrapur Thermal Power Project. Though
reduction of receivables from neighboring States may involve politically
sensitive issues, the recent deterioration in the situation and the risk of



-48-
MSEB increasingly delaying its own payments to other public and private
undertakings because of cash shortage (para 4.18) makes it necessary for
MSEB to act rapidly to reduce receivables from neighboring States.
Understanding was reached with MSEB that from September 30, 1989 onward,
late payments by neighbouring States will be subject to a 18Z penalty
interest surcharge, and that MSEB will make its best efforts to secure
confirmed letters of credit for any outstanding amount.
4.18     Commercial Payables.  MSEB purchases fuel from CIL and oil
companies; energy from Tata, NTPC and other States; and transport services
from Indian Railways. The average payment delay as of end-FY 1987/88 was
3.8 months of purchases, but it has been increasing alarmingly over the
past three years, creating financial difficulties with suppliers, including
power suppliers such as NTPC, most of which are also in a tight cash
situation. The recent trend should be redressed, and to that effect,
a-reement was reached during negotiations that MSEB will reduce commercial
payables to less than three and a half months of consumption (i.e. fuel and
consumables and purchases of power) by March 31, 1990; three months by
March 31, 1991; and two months from March 31, 1992 onward para 6.02(j)).
Investment Financing Policy
4.19     The Board's investment financing plan for the period FY 1987/88 to
FY 1994/95 is given in Table 4.5 below. Non-Government funding (market
bonds and commercial banks loans), are projected to increase by about 9Z
annually, from Rs 2.6 billionb in FY 1986/87 to Rs 4.6 billions in FY
1994/95, amounting to Rs 29.8 billions over the FY 1988/89 to 1994/95
period. Annual GOM loans will increase from Rs 1.3 billions in FY 1986/87
to about ks 14 billions in FY 1994/95, and will amount to Rs 55.5 billions
over the FY 1988/89 to FY 1994/95 period. wY .le MSEB will provide Rs 27
billions (23Z of financing requirements) from internally generated funds.

State Government Transfers
4.20     Although in the past the State Government contributed about 502 to
MSEB's investment, transfers from GOM to MSEB were negative over the last
three years, because capital outflows including State taxes and 0OM debt
service payments, exceeded capital inflows including new loans and
subsidies. The Board therefore did not burden State Government finances.
In the future, MSEB's increasing investment program will make the Board
more dependent on GOM's net transfers, which will reach Rs 4.1 billions by
FY 1992/93, despite increasing borrowings from non-government sources and
Rs 8.1 billion in FY 1994/95. It is in this context that resource
mobilization from consumers, through adequate tariff policy (para 4.13),
and increased ROR, to alleviate MSEB's reliance on GOM's bud-et, becomes
particularly important.

V. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
5.01     The economic analysis of the proposed project is reported fully in
Annex 5.1 and summarized below. Because India's power systems are planned
on a regional basis, economic analysis focuses on the Western Region power
system, where the proposed project is located.
Electricity Demand In The Western Region
5.02     Electricity consumption in the Western Region and projected
demands through 1999/2000 are summarized in Annex 5.1, Tables 1 and 2.
Between 1981/82 and 1987/88, the maximum load met increased at 11.4Z per
annum.  Through 1999/2000, CEA projects that maximum demand will increase
at 8.6Z per annum (Annex 5.1, para 3). Similarly, while energy consumption
through 1987/88 increased at 12.72 per annum, CEA projects growth through
1999/2000 will average 8.42 per annum. Demand growth is expected to be led
by domestic consumers (132 per annum), while growth in agricultural demand
is expected to ease (from 12.8Z per annum to 8.6Z per annum). Demand by
industry is expected to increase at 7.42 per annum, in line with current
growth. Even though generating capacity is projected to increase at 10.12
per annum, the projections suggest that in 1995/96 (when Koyna IV would be
commissioned), the Western Region will still face a shortage of generating
capacity of about 162.
5.03     Between 1981/82 and 1985/86, India's elasticity of electricity
consumption to economic growth was approximately 1.6. In the Western
Region, this elasticity was approximately 2.1, reflecting the high share of
industry in the Region's economic activity. Some reduction in the Region's
elasticity is likely (Annex 5.1, para 4). Even so, compared with GOI's
targets for economic growth of 62 per annum under the Eighth Plan and
growth of about 52 per annum thereafter, projected increases in electricity
demand in the Western Region of 8.42 per annum appear conservative. This
suggests therefore, that there is little likelihood that the Western Region
development program as presently planned will not be utilized fully upon
commissioning. The project analysis reported below suggests further that,
in the event that some adjustment of the program is required, deferral of
other projects would provide greater cost savings than deferral of Koyna
IV; hence that there is little likelihood of Koyna IV not being utilized
fully upon commissioning.
Least-Cost Analysis
5.04     CEA and the Bank have undertaken studies to examine: (i) the
justification for the inclusion of Koyna IV in the least-cost program for
the Western Region; and (ii) the optimum size of the project and proposed
date of commissioning (Annex 5.1, paras 5 to 8). Compared with alternative
projects that would provide approximately the same additional peak
generation capacity as Koyna IV, the analysis shows the program including
Koyna IV offers a cost advantage in terms of the present value of total



-51-.
system costs of approximately 8.6Z (about US$430 million). 11/ For Koyna
IV to lose this advantage, the costs of the project would have to triple,
or the fuel costs of thermal plant would have to decrease 27Z in real terms
(Annex 5.1, para 6). The analysis also confirms that the optimum capacity
of the project is 1000 MW (Annex 5.1. Table 4) and that to mximize
benefits, the project should be constructed as soon as possible (Annex 5.1,
Table 5). An installation of 750 MW instead of 1000 MW would increase the
present value of system costs by 2.6Z (about US$129 million), while a one-
year delay in commissioning would increase costs by 1.51 (about US$75
million).
Program and Prolect Analyses
5.05     Benefits that will be derived from the components of the proposed
project cannot easily be separated from benefits that will be derived from
other investments in generation, transmission and distribution in the
Western Region. Because of this, it is appropriate to focus cost-benefit
analysis primarily on a time-slice of the Western Region development
program as a whole. However, when prices are below marginal costs (as
power prices mostly are in India), demand can be inflated and can lead to
an investment program which is larger than can be justified purely on
economic grounds. In these circumstances, an acceptable program rate of
return can mask uneconomic projects. Analysis of the Western Region
program therefore has been supplemented with a separate analysis of Koyna
IV to ensure the project is economic, as well as the development program
time-slice as a whole. The time-slice analysed is 1989/90 to 1997/98.
This period covers implementation and the first two years operation of
Koyna IV and the period of implementation of most other new generation
projects to be started in the Western Region over the next few years.
Program and Proiect Costs
5.06     Capital and operating and maintenance costs of the 1989/90 to
1997/98 time-slice of the Western Region development program are shown in
Annex 5.1, Table 6. Corresponding costs for the Koyna IV project are shown
in Annex 5.1, Table 7. All costs have been converted to economic terms by
removing taxes and duties and by shadow-pricing local costs, using an SCF
of 0.8. Investments in transmission and distribution are not planned
integrally with generation. For the regional program, an allowance for
associated transmission and distribution investments has been included at
60Z of generation investments (Annex 5.1, para 11). For Koyna IV,
transmission investments are the costs of reinforcing the lines which
connect the existing Koyna station to bulk supply delivery points. For
ill These comparisons have allowed for the higher energy generation of
thermal alternatives to Koyna IV. As the Western Region is
predominantly short of power rather than energy (Annex 5.1, Table 1),
these additional benefits nostly accrue in the form of fuel cost
savings from displacing more costly thermal plant from operation.



-52-
associated distribution investments, a small allowance has been made in
proportion to the small amount of incremental energy that vill be provided
by Koyna IV (Annex 5.1, para 12). Operating and maintenance costs are
assumed to be 2.52 per annum of the capitals cost of coal-fired and gas-
fired plant, 1.13t per a*num of the capital costs of hydroelectric plant,
and 1.02 per annum of the capital costs of transmission and distribution
(Annex 5.1. para 13). Transmission and distribution losses are assumed to
be 15 of gross generation. New coal stations are assumed to consume
0.61 kg of coal and 15 ml of oil per kWh generated. The economic costs of
coal and oil are assumed respectively to be Rs 193 per tonne and Rs 3.36
per liter. Gas power stations are assumed on average to consume 0.23m3 per
kWh generated. The opportunity cost of gas is assumed to be Rs 1,920 per
thousand m3 (Annex 5.1. para 14).
Program and Project Benefits
5.07     Benefits that will be derived from the Western Region development
program mostly will be in the form of incremental electricity consumption.
Additional benefits may be realized through reducing supply costs during
off-peak periods and through improving the quality of supplies. However,
these are likely to be small by comparison and have not been factored into
either the program or project analyses (Annex 5.1, paras 15-16). Partly,
benefits of the development program can be measured by the incremental
revenues generated through the increase in consumption. Similarly, the
benefits of Koyna IV can partly be measured by the incremental revenues
that will be yielded through concentrating the station's generation into
peak periods. 12/ At present, the average tariff level in the Western
Region is estimated to be 87 paise per kWh. By 1995/96, it is expected to
reach the equivalent of 100 paise per kWh (in constant 1989/90 prices).
After 1995/96, the average tariff level has been assumed to remain constant
in real terms. Applying an SCF of 0.8, the corresponding economic value
associated with incremental consumption is assumed to be 70 paise per kWh
in 1989/90 rising to 80 paise per kWh in 1995/96 (Annex 5.1, para 18). The
incremental economic value of concentrating Koyna IV's generation into peak
periods also is estimated to be 80 paise per kWh (Annex 5.1. para 26).
5.08     The total economic benefits associated with incremental
consumption will be at least equal to what consumers are required to pay
for additional supplies (otherwise consumption would not increase). These
associated net benefits to consumers (consumezers' surplus) are directly
attributable to the increase in consumption facilitated by the development
program. In the program analysis, consumer surplus is estimated to be
extended to all categories of consumers. The rate of net benefit imputed
has been conservatively assumed to be half the savings consumers realize
through access to public electricity supply compared with the additional
costs of autogeneration (Annex 5.1, paras 19 to 22, and Table 10) In
economic terms, this rate of benefit is estimated to be 41 paise per kWh.
This brings estimated total economic benefits to 111 paise per kWh in
1989/90 rising to 121 paise per kWh in 1995/96. In the project analysis,
consumer surplus is estimated to be confined largely to peak period
consumers, which are domestic, commercial and agricultural consumers. On
the 302 of consumption accounted for by these consumers, surplus is
estimated to be 180 paise per kWh at 1989/90 prices (Annex 5.1, para 29).
12/ The analysis assumes that two-part tariffs will be in effect by the
time Koyna IV is commissioned.



-53-
Zconomic Rates of Return
Program
5.09     The estimated rates of return of the 1989190 to 1997198 time-slice
of the Western Region development program aret
ti) 7.2Z counting as benefits only incremental revenues; and
(ii) 14.0? when allowance is made for associated consumer surplus.
With a 122 discount factor, the NPV of the time-slice (including the
allowance for consumer surplus) is estimated at Rs 12,294 millions
(equivalent to US$820 millions, (Annex 5.1, para 30).
5.10     Even including the allowance for associated consumer surplus,
sensitivity analyses show the program could absorb adverse changes in costs
and benefits amounting to only about 10? before some reconfiguration of the
program would be required for it to remain economic (in the sense of
earning a return greater than 12?). Similarly, as presently configured,
the program could withstand an implementation delay of only one year. The
project analysis demonstrates that in the event some adjustment to the
Western Region program is required, Koyna IV would not be a preferred
candidate for deferral. It must also be remembered that set against these
adverse sensitivities are equal likelihoods of more favourable outcomes for
the development program.
Proiect
5.11     The estimated rates of return of the proposed Koyna IV project
(including associated investments in transmission and distribution) ares
(i) 9.52 counting as benefits only incremental revenues; and
tii) 18.3Z when allowance is made for associated consumer surplus.
Including associated consumer surplus, the NPV of the project is estimated
at Rs 2,891 millions (US$193 millions) (Annex 5.1, para 33).
5.12     Sensitivity analyses suggest that the returns (including consumer
surplus) yielded by Koyna IV are more robust than those yielded by the
program as a whole. The Koyna IV project could absorb adverse changes in
costs and benefits amounting to about 40? and remain economic. Similarly,
the project is able to withstand a delay in implementation of more than
three years. Variations greater than these are considered very unlikely.
Hence there is a strong likelihood that Koyna IV will prove economic. The
results demonstrate that deferral of other projects in the Western Region
program would provide greater economies than deferral of Koyna IV in the
event that some reconfiguration of the program became desirable (Annex 5.1,
para 34).

VI. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATION
Agreements Reached During Negotiations
6.01     During negotiations, agreement was reached with GOM:
(a) on the review of the technical aspects of Koyna IV by a panel
of experts in accordance with Terms of Reference agreed with
the Bank and that it will: (i) furnish for Bank review the
revised composition of the Panel of Experts including the
qualifications of the proposed members; and (ii) no later
than December 30, 1989, the roster from which POE members
will be selected and the qualifications of candidates
included in the roster, for comments (para 3.07);
(b)  on the appointment, before December 31, 1989 of an
independent international dam review panel to: (i) review the
present condition of Koyna dam; (ii) determine the scope of
additional investigations that may be required; (iii)
establish the need for specialized consultants; (iv) review
the result of the investigations and repaid.a of said dam
(para 3.08);
*c) that it will furnish to the Bank by December 31, 1990, a
report on the recommendation of panel mentioned in (d) above;
and, if recommended by the panel, retain consultants by
June 30, 1991, to assist GOM in implementing recommendations
of said panel (para 3.08);
(d) to revise suitably the organization of the project unit, and
to give to the Bank an opportunity to comment on any
substantial change in the organization arrangements for
project implementation (para 3.10);
(e) to furnish a report to the Bank before December 31, 1989, on
the individual cases of both land-owning and landless
families affected by construction of Koyna IV, including
applicability of the Maharashtra Project Displaced Persons
Act of 1976, and to compensate, on the basis of the report,
the families in accordance with the Act (para 3.14);
(f) that, if forest clearance for the access road to the surge
tank is not granted before December 31, 1989, GOMID will
consider alternative access (para 3.16); and
(g) to furnish to the Bank, no later than December 31, 1989, a
satisfactory timetable for key actions for implementation of
the transmission lines to connect Koyna IV to the Western
grid, and to implement the project in accordance with the
agreed timetable (para 3.25).

for Managerial Development (para 2.12);
(b)  that by December 31, 1989, MSEB will furnish to the Bank a
copy of the final report of the tariff study and afford to
the Bank an opportunity to comment on its proposed load

(c) to prepare and furnish to the Bank, no later than March 1st
of every year, a work-plan including the list of transmission
lines and substations to be financed by the Bank in the
following financial year, the list of contract packages for
which tenders would be invited, the associated timetable for

plan for persons displaced, based on actual surveys
(para 3.15);
(d)  that material and goods for the transmission and distribution
component (except the lines for Koyna IV) would be eligible
for Bank financing only if contracts are awarded before
December 31, 1992 (para 3.24);
Ce)  on the content and implementation of a Financial Action Plan
(para 4.01);
(f)  that it will take all necessary measures, including tariff
adjustments, to maintain its debt coverage ratio, defined as
cash generated from operations divided by debt service
accrued in the previous year, including interest during
construction, is above 1.00 in financial year 1989/90, and
1.20 from financial year 1990/91 onward (para 4.14);
(g)  that MSEB's financial rate of return after interest (GOI
definition) will be no less than 3Z in financial year 1989/90
and 1990/91; 3.52 in financial year 1991/92 and 1992/93; and
42 from financial year 1993/94 onward (para 4.15);
(h)  to discuss annually with the Bank, by December 31 of eacb
year, revisions to its five-year revolving capital investment
and financing Plan (para 4.16);
(i)  to reduce total commercial receivables less any disputed
amount to less than 2.5 months of revenues from March 31,
1990 onward (para 4.17); and

Recommendation
6.03     On the basis of the proje~:t justification and the agreements to be

Page 1 of 4
INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Action Plan for Managerial Development
1. INTRODUCTION
1.1      MSEB has experienced in the past five years an unprecedented
growth, both in term of power generation, with an annual generation
increasing from 15,000 GWh in 1983 to 23,000 GWh in 1988, and in term of
number of consumers, from 4.1 million in 1983, to 6.4 million in 1988,
accompanied by a considerable expansion and increase in Transmission and
Distribution system complexity. Simultaneously, responsibilities are
increasingly decentralized at the operational unit level, with a
corresponding need for more coordination between operational and functional
units.
1.2      In consideration of the anticipated continuation of MSEB's growth
(MSEB will generate 39,000 GWh in 1995, and serve 10 million consumers),
MSEB has decided to modernize its organization, and to pursue the
decentralization of responsibilities by strengthening its Management
Information Systems (MIS).
1.3      The modernization of MSEB's organization will take place
gradually, building up on the existing organization, which was amended in
FY 1987/88. The objectives of the reorganization are:
(i)   to reflect in the organization the increasing
importance of the Distribution function;
(ii)  to strengthen coordination between technical and
managerial functions for corporate planning
purpose; and
(iii) to improve and streamline internal communication
and procedures for operations and management.
1.4      In parallel, MSEB's MIS will be strengthened to support
the reorganization, by:
(i)   improving the quality of technical and managerial
information;
(ii)  accelerating processing and dissemination of
relevant information;
(iii) improving communication amongst key technical and
managerial functions; and
(iv)  to improve industrial risk management.



-65-                            Annex 2.3
Page 2 of 4
2. ORGANIZATION
2.1 Target:   -  Strengthen Distribution.
Actionss  -  The purpose of the re-organization of the Technical
function is to strengthen Distribution planning. In order
to achieve this objective, MSEB will prepare by June 30,
1990, a plan with an implementing timetable, and implement
it thereafter (Attachment 1).
2.2 Target:   -  Improve internal coordination.
Actions:  - MSEB will establish by June 30, 1990 a Corporate Planning
Unit directly under the authority of the Chairman. This
unit will be in charge of: (i) translating the corporate
strategy into corporate plan objectives; (ii) coordination
of planning amongst the main functions, and preparation
and updating of MSEB's Corporate Plan (Five-, Ten-, and
Twenty-year revolving plans); and (iill) coordination with
CEA, GOM, and WREB for the insertion of MSEB's plans in
the more general State, Regional and National Plans
(Attachment 2).
- MSEB will establish by the same date Planning Units in the
Technical (Generation & Transmission, and Distribution &
Commercial), and Administrative sectors, directly under
the relevant Members. These units will be responsible for
the preparation and updating of their respective
components of the Corporate Plan. They will work in close
relation with the Central Planning Unit.
-  The existing Planning Unit in the Accounting sector will
be strengthenedt qualified professional staff will be
assigned to the unit, and data processing equipment will
be procured by June 30, 1990.
- MSEB will establish by June 30, 1990 a centralized Data
Processing Department in charge of coordination of data
processing development in MSEB, development of in-house
softwares, operation of the mainframe computer; and
management of MSEB's data processing network
(Attachment 3).
2.3 Target:   -  Streamline Internal Procedures.
Actions:  - MSEB will establish by March 31, 1990, an Operation and
Maintenance (OEM) Unit under Member Technical. This unit
will be in charge of establishing an Operations Management
System (OMS) and technical performance indicators;
defining standard reporting requirements and formats on
operation and maintenance activities activities. The O&M
unit will monitor the timeliness and quality of reports;
and organizing the circulation of information. The head
and key officers of the new unit will be sent on an



-66-                            Annex 2.3
Page 3 of 4
information mission to selected public utilities abroad to
study modern OMS systems, and design a system to meet
MSEB's needs (Attachment 4).
- MSEB will prepare and enforce, by December 31, 1990,
maintenance manuals and establish maintenance routines for
buildings, auxiliary services (water treatment plants,
repair shops, storage areas, etc.) for which no
manufacturers' manuals exist. Manuals and preventive
maintenance routines will cover power plants, substations,
stores, shops and field offices.
-  By the same date, MSEB will expand the existing Internal
Control Unit, presently under the Member Accounts and in
charge of financial control, into an Internal Audit Unit,
in charge of auditing all internal procedures, aud
compliance of all departments with procedures for
financial transactions, operations, personnel management,
etc. Terms of Reference for the new unit, and its
location in MSEB's organization will be decided upon by
June 30, 1990 (Attachment 5).
2.4 Target:   -  Improve Risk Management.
Actions:  - MSEB will make suitable administrative and organizational
arrangements by December 31, 1990 to assign responsibility
for industrial risk evaluation, monitoring and insurance
coverage, as well as for establishment and implementation
of safety procedures within and without MSEB, to be
effective by December 31, 1990 (Attachment 8).
2.5 Target:   - Monitoring MSEB's compliance with environment standards.
Actions:  - MSEB will establish by June 30, 1990, an Environmental and
Forest Affairs Unit to coordinate and monitor
environmental, forestry and resettlement work in MSEB,
under the Board Member (Technical) (Attachment 9).
- MSEB will prepare by the same date a long-term training
program for the staff of the newly created unit.
3. MANAGEMENT INFORMATION SYSTEMS (MIS)
3.1 Target:   -  Improve the quality of technical and managerial
information.
Actions:  - MSEB will implement an OMS and establish a set of
performance indicators (2.3 above). The selection of
suitable performance indicators will be based on
experience in selected public utilities. The definition
of OMS and performance indicators will be completed by
June 30, 1990, and their implementation will be completed
by June 30, 1991.



-67-                           Annex 2.3
Page 4 of 4
- MSEB vill modernize its management accounting system for
Generation, transmission, and Distribution, to provide
more detailed information, and develop cost analysis for
management purpose, based on the Direct Costing method
(Attachment 3). The design of the new system should be
completed by June 30. 1990 and it: implementation by
June 30, 1991 (Attachment 7).
3.2 Target:   - Accelerate data processing expansion.
Actions:  - MSEB will expand its training program for data processing,
and provide training for at least 100 persons every year
from FY 1990191 onward.
- MSEB will prepare by December 1990 a data processing plan
covering the gradual development of the Data Processing
Department, the Board's policy regarding the relative role
of centralized data processing, regional computer centers,
and micro-computers, the development plan for the Board's
mainframe computer and associated centralized
applications, the plan and schedule for the extension of
the computerized billing system already in place in four
zones, to the remaining three, and the plan for the
dissemination of micro-computers for management. The plan
will include annual budget estimates for hardware and
softwares acquisition, and evaluations of the volume of
inputsioutputs to be processed in the future.
- In relation with the data processing plan, MSEB will
prepare a detailed assessment of the employment and social
impact of the envisaged computerization, to be presented
with the data processing plan, by December 31, 1990.
3.3 Target:   -  Improve coordination and communication.
Actions:  - The new Planning Unit (2.2 above) will prepare a five-year
revolving corporate plan by December 1990.
- The generation, transmission and distribution departments
will prepare by the same date a five-year plan for
operations, transmission and distribution.
- The personnel department vill prepare by the same date a
plan for human resources development (Attachment 7).
including monitorable personnel productivity indicators
and targets, a manpower plan, a training program, a staff
redeployment plan, and an action plan for staff
motivation.
- The planning units in the technical departments will
prepare by the same date a consistent generation,
transmission and distribution investment and investment
funding plan.
- The Accounting Department will prepare by the same date a
financial plan consistent vith the operation, investment
and personnel plans.

INDIA
Sjk4NASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Suggested Terms of Reference for Strengthening MSEB Capabilities
for Planning and Design of Transmission and Distribution Systems
Obiective
1.    The objective of the proposed assistance is to strengthen MSEB
capabilities to plan and design least cost electricity distribution systems
by: i) setting up a computerized system in MSEB for planning and design of
distribution systems including the corresponding data management system; ii)
training of MSEB staff in planning and design of distribution systems, in
operation and use of the computerized system and in collection and maintenance
of the data base nee-ded for this purpose; iii) reviewing and revising if
necessary design standards and practices for distribution systems presently
used in MSEB to insure that resulting plans are least cost; and, iv) assist
MSEB in revising design procedures and manuals for distribution systems.
Scone of Consultancv Work
2.    The consultant, after assessing MSEB needs and data availability would
develop or adapt a computerized system for establishing the distribution
system data base, analyzing existing loss levels and analyzing alternative
expansion strategies for distribution systems. The consultant should determine
the quality of data being used and recommend any improvements needed to
correct existing deficiencies.
3.    The consultant should provide the software and assist MSEB in the
acquisition of computers and peripherals needed to establish the distribution
data base and the proposed planning system. The hardware requirements include
micro-computers complete with adequate storage and display facilities,
printers, a digitizer for data entry of maps and drawings and a plotter for
producing circuit maps and circuit diagrams. The software should drive the
above equipment and should include as a minimum the following: (a) a load
forecasting program cable of using basic data such as land use
classifications, population growth, etc. to develop forecasts; (b) a
distribution study program to provide load flows, voltage profiles, sectional
losses and total losses; (c) a transformer load management program; (d) a
circuit analysis program. All of the programs should interface with a common
data base and ideally should be capable of utilizing data directly from a
future billing system. The consultant is responsible for the complete
installation and interfacing of the computer software and hardware. The
consultant should indicate the guarantee maintenance period for the system and
any other follow-up services.
4.    The consultant should train key staff selected by MSEB in the use of the
software and hardware and in modern approaches and technology for p. nning and
design of distribution systems, including setting up and maintenance of data



Annex 2.3
-69-                           Attachment 1
base. Training would be imparted through formal classroom conferences, on the
job by developing actual plans for at least two of the cities contemplated in
the proposed Bank financed project, and through structured study tours to
utilities abroad. The consultants should as part of their proposals furnish a
specific training section explaining their planned approach to it.
5.    The consultant should undertake a critical review of MSEB current
standards, practices and technical specifications for key materials and
equipment for distribution systems and recommend in a report changes to be
introduced to make designs more reliable, economic and a tune with current
international practice. The consultant would also propose the outline, if
necessary, of distributing a planning and design manual to be developed and
implemented by MSEB. The consultant would review and comment on the manual
once finalized.
6.    The consultants should furnish MSEB with full documentation on the
computer planning system for its operation and maintenance.

MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Action Plan for Managerial Development
Proposed Responsibilities for a Corporate Planning Unit in MSEB
Obiective
1.       The Corporate Planning Unit (CPU) will ensure that the plans
developed by each function adequately reflect MSEB's overall strategy and
are consistent with each other. MSEB will have plans for the medium and
long-term (5, 10 and 15 years), which will be adjusted and rolled over
every year.
Placement
2.       The CPU will be established directly under the authority of the
Chairman of MSEB. In addition, MSEB's main directorate will establish
their own planning units working closely with Lhe central unit.
Tasks
The CPU will: (i) develop and communicate corporate objectives for
the main subsectors in MSEB; (ii) monitor and coordinate preparation of
decentralized plans by each of the main functions in MSEB (generation,
transmission, distribution, marketing, finance, personnel, data
processing); (iii) integrate decentralized plans into a comprehensive and
consistent overall plan for MSEB; and (iv) monitor implementation of plans
and timely achievement of specified targets by each of the main functions.
Essential stages in this process are:
(a) Environment Analysis and Forecast
- Examine economic, regulatory and political influence.
- Examine technology.
- Examine market outlook.
- Identify and evaluate key external factors.
- Prepare environmental forecast.
(b) Competitive Analysis and Position Assessment
- Analyze current position in the industry.
- Develop industry strategic success factors.
- Identify and evaluate MSEB's strengths and weaknesses.
- Summarize position in the industry.
(c) Obiectives and Goals Development
Define business mission.
- Develop objectives, goals and strategic success factors.
- Communicate objectives, goals and strategic success
factors.



-71-                          Annex 2.3
Attachment 2
(d) Formulation of Alternative Strategies Abouts
- Service and consumer markets.
- Operation efficiency.
- Organizational structure.
- Technology to be used.
4.       The CPU will develop MSEB's corporate plan, based on the strategy
chosen, and ensure that goals and objectives are clearly communicated to
the implementing units, management, personnel are adequately trained, and
adequate incentive systems are developed to support the new business plan.
Human and Technical Resources
5.       The CPU will consist of a small number (five to seven) of high
level officers with experience of each functional unit, and headed by a
senior officer with extensive experience within MSEB, preferably with
technical or economic background. A continuity of employment in these
functions will be needed, since a CPU memory will be important. Use and
experience of computers is essential.
Timeframe
6.       The CPU will be established no later than December 31, 1989.  An
early establishment will facilitate the integration of the Eighth Plan in
MSEB's plan.

-72-                        Attachment 3
INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Action Plan for Managerial Development
Data Processing Plan
Obiective
The objective of the data processing plan will be to design future
development in the data processing system of NSEB, based on estimated needs
and assessment of costslbenefits to be expected from alternative
strategies.
Scope of Work
The consultant should:
(I) Existing Situation
(a)  assess existing data processing equipments in HSEB, their rate of
utilization, and cast a judgment on their adequacy for the jobs
and efficiency in utilization;
(b) assess the existing organization for data processing in MSEB
including organization and efficiency of department(s) in charge
of computer operation, software maintenance, and software
development;
(c) audit the existing computerized applications with regard to their
adequacy for their intended purpose, end-user satisfaction with
output, quality and efficiency of application analyses and
progrnimning, and quality of supporting documentation; and
(d) assess the volume of processing required for each application in
terms of inputs and outputs, and computer memory and configuration
requirements.
(II) Estimation of Future Needs
(a) Prepare, in collaboration with future end-users, a list and brief
definition of possible future computer applications for
management;
(b) assess for each application the envisaged volume of processing and
computer configuration requirements at S and 10 years horizons;
(c) prepare for each application a brief cost/benefit analysis; and
(d) propose priority ranking for the envisaged applications.



Annex 2.3
-73-                          Attachment 3
(III) Development Plan
(a) Propose alternative computerization strategies and computer system
architectures based on (III) above;
(b) prepare suitable phasing for each of the envisaged alternative;
(c)  prepare cost estimates for each strategy, including hardware,
software conversion, development and creation, logistic needs,
personnel direct cost and training cost, and possible staff
redeployment costs;
(d)  make recommendations for an optimum strategy, based on cost-
benefit and risk analyses, taking into account existing
constraints in the local context.
(IV)  Implementation Program
(a)  Once HSEB's management has selected a specific data processing
development strategy, prepare a feasible five-year development
plan and implementation program, including financial requirements
and human resources requirements; and
(b) prepare a detailed training program for MSEB staff.
Reporting
The consultants will prepare a diagnostic report at the end of
phase I. A report on the scope for compaterization in HSEB will be
prepared at the end of phase II. The report on phase III, including the
presentation of alternative strategies and the recommended strategy, should
present at least four feasible alternatives. The report on phase IV will
be a detailed implementation plan, with specifications for the proposed
equipment for the first five years of the program, and proposed actions for
the organization of computer department(s) in HSEB.
Timing
The diagnosis should be presented four weeks after inctption of
the study.
The second phase should start after HSEB has commented on the
diagnosis, some six months after issuance of phase I diagnosis. The second
phase should last for 12 weeks. The review of phase II report by HSEB
would take about six weeks.
The third phase would take about four weeks. The review of the
phase III report and the selection of a strategy for HSEB would take
16 weeks.
Phase IV would take 12 weeks.



Annex 2.3
-74-                       Attachment 3
Study Team
MSEB will establish a working group for the preparation of the
data processing plan, associating representative of the main departments
(about six persons) under the chairmanship of the officer-in-charge of
corporate planning in MSEB. The working group will steer the study and
interface as necessary with the consultant.
MSEB will also delegate at least two junior high level staff to
work closely on a daily basis with the consultant.
The consultant team should include at least one senior staff
experienced in the power sector, within a team of three to five computer
and management specialists, one of which should be experienced with data
processing training, and one with computer systems in large public
utilities in developed countries.

Proposed Responsibilities for an
Operation Maintenance System (OMS) Unit in MSEB
Objectives
The objectives of the OMS unit are to:
(a) improve technical effectiveness and cost efficiency of generation,
transmission and distribution;
(b) improve maintenance standards; and
*c) strengthen managerial accountability.
Placement
The unit will report to the Board Member (Technical). Corresponding
units will be established under the Directors Generation, Distribution,
Marketing (if needed], and for new projects.
Responsibilities
The main responsibilities of the unit will be to:
I. With regard to Performance Monitoring
(a) establish and maintain a system of performance monitoring
indicators in MSEB, based on the World Bank manual "Proposals for
Monitoring the Performance of Electric Utilities", adapted as
necessary;
(b) ensure that performance indicators are analyzed, discussed, and
trigger suitable corrective actions;
(c)  establish and update technical consumption and efficiency norms in
MSEB; and
(d) identify and propose cost-saving measures and improvement to
working practices.
II. With regard to Operation Maintenance
(a) set guidelines for MSEB regarding safety at work, and procedures
for performing routine tasks;



-76-                    Annex 2.3
Attachment 4
(b) establish and monitor preventive maintenance staniards; and
(c) prepare and maintain up-to-date maintenance manuals for
generations.
Proposed Human and Technical Resources
The central OMS unit should comprise one section for operation
mainten'..tce, and one section for performance monitoring.  It should have a
complement of at least five engineers, preferably with experience in
maintenance, operations and management.
The units in the main technical directorates should comprise about
three engineers each, depending on the technical requirements.
The units should be providdw ,ith substantial data processing support,
inter-connected with the cost a_counting data base.  Data processing
requirements will be defined under the Data Processing Plan.
Timing for Implementation
(a) Establishment of central OMS units December 31, 1989.
(b)  Appointment of staffs March 31, lg'.
(c) Design of maintenance standards, preparation of maintenance
manuals, and performance indicators: June 30, 1990.
(d) Implementation: June 30, 1990 to March 31, 1991.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Action Plan for Managerial Development
Proposed Responsibilities for an Internal Audit Unit in MSEB
Obiective
The objective of the unit is to establish and safeguard the plan
of organization and all of the coordinate methods and measures adopted
within MSEB to safeguard its assets; check the accuracy and reliability of
its accounting data; promote operational efficiency; encourage adherence to
prescribed managerial policies; and monitor compliance with existing
guidelines and internal managerial procedures.
Placement
The Internal Audit Unit should report directly to the Chairman of
MSEB, and be fully autonomous in its investigations.
Responsibilities
The unit's main responsibilities will be:
A. Accountina Controls:
(1) Planning of organization and review of the procedures and records
concerned with the safeguarding of MSEB's assets and the
reliability of financial reports.
(2) Ensuring that accounting transactions are executed in accordance
with managemente's general or specific authorization.
(3) Ensuring financial transactions are recorded as necessary to
maintain accountability of assets and permit preparation of
financial statements.
(4) Ensuring that access to MSEB's assets is permitted only in
accordance with management authorization.
(5) Ensuring that recorded assets are compared with the existing
assets and appropriate action is taken with respect to any
difference.
B. Management Controls
(1) Monitor changes in the plan of organization of MSEB and in
allocation of responsibilities amongst units.



-78-                       Annex 2.3
Attachment 5
(2) Monitor compliance with procedures and records that are concerned
with the decision process leading to management's authorization of
transaction, in particular with regard to personvel management,
procurement, purchases, commercial, and safety.
Human and Technical Resources
The unit should be headed by a person with extensive experience in
accounting at a high level, preferably a chartered accountant. Staffing
should be at least four professional staff with training and experience in
accounting or operations, and at least five years of professional
experien-ce with MSEB. No computer support is required.
Timing for Implementation
Final terms of reference for the unit will be prepared by
December 31, 1989. Job description for professional staff will be prepared
by January 30, 1990.
The unit will be fully staffed and effective by March 31, 1990.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POlWER PROJECT
Action Plan for Managerial Development
Proposed Terms of Reference for the
Rehabilitation and Management Accounting System
Purpose
The purpose of the rehabilitation of the Management Accounting
System is to revise and expand MSEB's management accounting system so that
it covers all of MSEB's activity; provides meaningful data for management
of operational unit; provides basic data for tariff setting purpose and
calculation of long run marginal cost; and provides historical cost inputs
for annual budgeting. Timely dissemination of relevant information to
concerned managers will be emphasized in the new system design.
Preparation and Implementation
The revised management accounting system will be devised by a
specially appointed task force of no more than six members, under the
chairmanship of the Director of Finance. It will comprise representatives
of the major technical departments and of the financial complex. The task
force will also supervise the implementation of the revised system.
The task force will operate as a 'workshop' and prepare a full
design and implementation schedule for the revised system. In order to
discharge its responsibility in the most professional manner, and to help
introduce modern management accounting practices in MSEB, the task force
will, as necessary, draw on the expertise of exterral consultants for
specific tasks or advice. Moreover, the members of the task force will
study in deteal existing management accounting system in other public
utilities in India or abroad. The members of the task force will have the
possibility to visit selected public utilities, to examine in detail their
management accounting systems and draw on their experience to design MSEB's
system. The cost of these visits can be financed under the World BDank
Maharashtra Power Project. Each of these visits is expected to last about
two weeks in each utility.
Genera. Characteristics of the Envisaged System
(a) The system will collect and process cost data and also relevant
statistical data on the physical activity of each cost center in
MSEB, for the calculation of unit costs and cost performance
indicators for each center.
(b) The system will classify cost data, from the collection stage,
according to the following categories:
- by function in MSEB (generation, transmission, distribution,
marketing, overheads, capital investment, etc.);



-80-                         Annex 2.3
Attachment 6
- by nature (personnel, consumable, services, etc.);
- in relation with each cost center's main activity (direct,
indirect, overheads);
- in relation with their variability with the level of activity.
(c) Physical elements for cost calculation will be collecled in the
field from the team supervisors, to be quantified in monetary
terms by applying standard unit costs.
(d) The management accounting system will be fully reconciled with the
financial accounting system at the end of each financial year, and
operate on a standard cost basis during the year.
(e)  The system will operate on a quarterly basis; processed data will
be made available to managers within three weeks of the end of
each quarter.
(f) Specific formats for cost data presentation will be prepared for
each manager concerned. Each format will be designed in close
collaboration with future users.
(g) The management accounting system will comprise, in addition to
collection and processing syste-s, a plan for dissemination of
cost data amongst managers, an4 procedures for internal analysis
and discussion of cost data.
(h) The system will be suitably designed to permit calculation of cost
performance indicators for upper management level; detailed power
supply cost by category of consumer and period of the yearlday;
and standard unit costs for annual budget preparation.
Time Schedule
- Nomination of Task Force members: December 31, 1989.
- Departure for study of management accounting system abroad:
March 31, 1990.
- Recommendations of Task Force: August 31, 1990
- Detailed design of proposed system: December 31, 1990
- Implementation: January 1, 1991 to December 31, 1991.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Terms of Reference
Human Resources Development Plan
Obiective
The objectives of the plan are: (i) to find a better adequacy
between MSEB's staff and its manpower requirements; (ii) to limit further
increases in MSEB's work force to the minimum technically necessary; and
(iii) to design a recruitment and training facility in the policy to
satisfy MSEB's qualitative and quantitative long-term work force needs.
Methodology
For the preparation of the Human Resources Development Plan, MSEB
will:
(i) prepare detailed job descriptions for each of the positions in
the existing organization, with a particular emphasis on
previous experience and technical skill requirements, and
prepare standard career development paths;
(ii) prepare an inventory of MSEB's human resources, aiming at
evaluating the match between job description and existing human
resources;
(iii) prepare personnel policy objectives and a detailed estimate for
the next five and ten years of future personnel requirements
for each of the functions in MSEB, existing or to be created;
the estimate will comprise quantitative estimate of staffing
requirements and also skills requirements for the most
significant positions:
(iv) translate future personnel requirements in terms of labor
productivity based on activity indicators developed
independently for each of the main functions in MSEB;
(v) revise MSEB's training programs and courses based on skills
requirements of the job description as a result of (i) and
(iii) above, and of career development paths;
(vi)  prepare a training plan to adjust MSEB's personnel to their
present and future jobs;
(vii) prepare a long term quantitative staffing plan by function; and
(viii)  prepare a recruitment and transfer plan to satisfy future
needs.



-82-
Annex 2.3
Attachment 7
Responsibilities for the Preparation of the Plan
The Human Resources Development Plan will be prepared as part of
the Corporate Plan. It will be prepared by the Personnol Department. in
collaboration with the technical and management departments.
Timing
The complete planning process should be repeated every five years,
to be updated annually on revolving basis. The ten-year plan vill be
updated every five years.
The preparation of the first plan will commence in September 1989;
the p-eparation phase will be completed within twelve months for the first
plan. Annual update should take no more than three months, from September
to December, for incorporation in the annual update HSEB's overall
corporate plan.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWEP. PROJECT
Draft Terms of Reference for a Risk Management for HSEB
Introduction
1.         4MSEB is responsible for the public supply of electricity in
Maharashtra.  These Terms of Reference outline the purpose and scope of
tasks required to prepare a risk management and insurance program for
MSEB including recommendations to strengthen MSEB safety practices.
Obiectives
2.          The consultants should perform a risk analysis for MSEB and
propose a plan to minimize and manage M'EB's exposure. The consultants should
particularly:
a. identify risks and quantify them;
b. develop alternatives for risk management and recommend the best
options for MSEB including insurance practices as appropriate;
c. recommend internal administrative organization for managing
HSEB's risks;
d. determine the accounting treatment of premia and of losses and
risk not covered by external insurance;
e. determine the cover and type of policies to be maintained; and
f. determine the method of contracting insurance in the most
economical manner.
3.          In addition, the consultant would review established plant
inspection and safety procedures followed by MSEB, comparing them with broader
practices similar to the ones in well established utilities and to the
periodic inspection services generally provided by insurance companies as part
of their coverage. The consultant would then make suitable recommendations
for the establishment of inspection and safety procedures designed to
complement existing practices through the adoption of a full scope of
inspections and follow-up as would be required for insurance purposes.



-84-
Annex 2.3
Attachment 8
Scope of the Work
4.          The consultants should proceed in three phases.  It is important
that MSEB staff actively participate in each phase of works. The first 8t age
of the work requires a detailed ar.alysis of the principal risks to which kSEB
is exposed and a quantification of them. In the case of fixed assets, such
valuation should be made on replacement value, less corresponding
depreciation, except when the cost of replacement may be difficult to
determine, in which case they should take original cost less depreciation and
apply indices of inflation which are most appropriate to the class of such
assets. At the same time, they should make, for each risk, an economic
analysis to determine the most cost effective management technique for that
particular risk. Once the first phase has been completed a report will be
prepared giving the consultants' conclusions and recommendations.
5.          During the first phase the consultants should also:
a. review and evaluate the existing MSEB's inspection schedules
and procedures for generating stations and bulk substations
particularly in relation to planned maintenance and periodic
testing;
b. review and evaluation of existing MSEB's safety practices to
reduce hazardous conditions that may exist in operating and
maintaining the MSEB;s power system;
c. assist MSEB in establishing procedures for periodic inspections
of plant and equipment to enhance the flow of information on
status and capabilities of plant to the management;
d. assist MSEB in organizing safety seminars and developing
accident investigations and reporting leading to effective
accident prevention; and
e. assist MSEB in preparation of manuals and instructions for
safety oriented operation and maintenance procedures if
necessary.
6.          In the second stage the consultants should:
a. develop criteria for evaluating decisions concerning external
or self-insurance;
b. develop criteria for evaluation of risks to be covered and for
determining the nature and scope of such cover;
c. recommend the location of the insurance office in the
organization of MSEB, its duties and responsibilities and its
personnel requirements;



-85-
Annex 2.3
Attachment 8
d. determine the qualifications required for the personnel
responsible for decisions concerning insurance, and any needs
for training in insurance matters; and
e. develop internal administrative guidelines for contracting
insurance.
7.          The third stage of the study will be to estimate the cost of
insurance, whether it can be placed locally or overseas. If possible, it
would be helpful to obtain information on premia paid by other electric power
companies in other countries including developing ones. In the light of such
information, the consultants should make their recommendations on the amount,
type of cover and other characteristics of the insurance required by MSEB.
Such recommendations will be contained in the final report which will
comprise, inter alia, the following subjects:
a. procedures to be followed for contracting in the insurance; and
b. treatment to be followed in cases where it is decided to opt
for self-insurance, in particular:
i.   criteria for the assessment of amounts to be set aside
annually for the self-insurance;
ii. guidelines for the accounting of loss and damages; and
iii. rules for investment of the reserves.
8.          The consultant shall prepare an instruction manual to implement
all those recommendations resulting from his review.
9.          The consultant should advise MSEB as necessary to procure the
agreed insurance.
10.         The consultant shall prepare a final summary report listing all
aspects of the work and results achieved.

Annex 3.1
