INDI
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Detailed Project Description
A. Koyna IV Hydroelectric Power Station
Location
1.          The Koyna IV Hydroelectric powerplant would be located in
Maharashtra State, forming a parallel development to the existing first and
second stages which draw water from LaRe Shivajisagar, the reservoir of Koyna
Dam, and divert it wep"cward to power stations located at the western foot of
the Western Ghats. Koyna Dam is located 56 km west of Karad wl.ich is on the
Pune Bangalore road, and the power station site is located 25 km from Chiplun
which is on the coastal road from Bombay to Goa.  The dam is situated about
350 km from Bombay (Map IBRD 21492).
Development Concept
2.          The existing first and second stages of the Koyna development,
consist of an intake on Lake Shivajisagar, supplying water via twin headrace
tunnels and inclined shafts to an underground powerhouse, in which are
installed eight Pelton type generating units with a total installed capacity
of 56i SW.  From the powerhouse a tailrace tunnel leads to Lake Kolkewadi
which is the forebay of Koyna Stage III which provides a further 320 MW of
generating capacity. The Stage I and II units were commissioned over the
period 1962 to 1967, while Stage III units were conmissioned in 1975-76.
3.          The first and second stages were designed to operate on base load
with a plant load factor of 60 percent. While plarned reduced diverA.ions over
the period up until 1994 (para 6) would reduce the average plant load factor
to about 44 percent there is still scope for a further reduction in plant load
factor by the addition of further generating units. This opportunity arises
because of the growth of the interconnected system and the reductd proportion
of hydro plant in the overall generation mix of the Western Region. This is
the basis of the Koyna IV development concept.
4.          The proposed Stage IV development parallels the existing first ard
second stages, also developing the full 521 m head between Lake Shivajisagar
and Lake Kolkewadi. However the routing of the new power conduits would be
somewhat more direct than that of the existing development, which was designed
to be implemented in stages. An additional four 250 MW units would be
installed in the new underground powerhouse, but no additional flow would be
diverted. Instead the combined development would be assigned peak load duty
with plant load factor ccrresponding to reduced diversions equal to about 17
percent.



-94-
Annex 3. 1
Page 2 of 11
PhvsiograDhy and Hydrology
5.          The Koyna River originates in the Sahyadri ranges at an altitude
of about 1230 m and flows southwards parallel to the ridge line of the Western
Chats for some 65 km. At this location it turns sharply and flows eastwards
for a further 56 km where it joins the Rive- Krishna which eventually
discharges into the Bay of Bengal. The Koyna Dam is located some two
kilometers upstream of the point where the river turns towards the east. The
catchment upstream of the dam has an area of 892 km2. Because of its location
at the top of the steep scarp and orientation parallel to it, it receives a
very high annual rainfall averaging about 6000 mm over its area. About eighty
percent of the inflow occurs during the monsoon season which extends over the
period from June to October.
6.          Diversion of Koyna water has implications with respect to
downstream users, particularly since the Krishna River passes through two
other Indian states (Karnataka and Andra Pradesh) after leaving Maharashtra.
Permissible diversions from the upper Krishna by Maharashtra have baen laid
down by the Krishna Water Disputes Tribunal, which was set up by the
Government of India to adjudicate in this area. The decision of this tribunal
provides that diversions by Maharashtra from the upper Krishna shall be
gradually reduced to an annual volume of 67.5 million cubic feet (1911 hm3 )
by June 1, 1994. The calculation of economic benefits for the Koyna IV scheme
allows for this reduction.
7.          A second constraint on diversions from the Koyna is imposed by the
need to provide for downstream irrigation withdrawals within Maharashtra.
These amount to 729 hm3 annually and evaporation losses amount to 120 hm3.  If
these requitements plus full allowable diversions are to be met, inflow plus
carry over storaga from the previous year should exceed 2760 hm3.
Records of inflow into Koyna reservoir are available for the period since
impoundment in 1961 until the present. Prior to impoundment, river flow
records are available back until 1949. The overall period of records is
therefore about 40 years. Reservoir simulations over this period indicate
that full requirements would have been met in all years except 1972/73 and
1987/88, inferring a reliability of about 95 percent. Considering that this
reliability applies to both power and irrigation requirements, it can be
considered to be very high since acceptable irrigation rehabilities are
generally in the order of 80 percent.
Geology
8.          The whole of the Project would be constructed in Deccan Trap
basalts. The general characteristics of this formation are well known since
it covers 75 percent of the area of Maharashtra. The earlier stages of the
Koyna development were also located entirely in Deccan Trap basalts.
Throughout this formation, there is considerable spatial variation on a micro
scale, because it is made up of a large number of small lava flows. However,
because of the similarity of the formation process in each case the number of



-95-
Annex 3.1
Page 3 of 11
rock types is low and the characteristics of similar rock types from various
locations are relatively uniform.
9.          The primary rock types present in the Koyna area are vesicular,
amygdaloidal and compact basalts. Volcanic breccias and red boles also occur
but in relatively narrow bands. The vesicular and amygdaloidal basalts are
relatively weak but because of their massive structure provide an ideal
tunnelling medium. The compact non-vesicular basalts are always well jointed
which is reflected in increased overbreaks, but temporary support during
tunnelling is rarely required even when tunnel dimensions are large. The
volcanic breccias are relatively weak and temporary support would generally be
required. The red boles are probably the only rock type which could be
classified as poor. However because they occur only in thin bands, they
provide only a minor impediment to tunnelling.
10.         There is considerable knowledge of the rock types in which the
K-yna IV underground excavations will be situated. This has been gained from
thie construction experience from the previous stages supplemented by site
investigations and preliminary works carried out for the presently proposed
development stage. During construction of the earlier srages, temporary
support of excavations was only rarely required. No significant or unexpected
geological problems were encountered during construction. The access tunnel
and a number of other tunnels associated with the powerhouse remain unlined
and urnsupported 25 years after construction. As part of the preliminary works
for tne present stage more than a kilometer of large diameter tunnel has been
excavated with no support except an occasional rock bolt. Water inflows are
insignificant, even for excavations which are below the water level of the
existing lakes.
11.         Although the overall characteristics of the formation are well
known, extensive additional site investigations have been carried out for the
current stage including some 25 diamond drill holes. These have been
generally aimed at obtaining a detailed knowledge in the area of the more
critical structures. Overall it can be considered that geological conditions
at the site are favorable to the construction of an underground development
and that the level of knowledge of the geology is high.
PowerDlant Layout
12.         A new intake would be constructed at the edge of the reservoir.
The intake bellmouth for Koyna IV has been fixed at a higher elevation than
that of the existing bellmoutas for Koyna I and II and it is planned to
operate the reservoir during construction, such that it is drawn down to below
the new intake level at the end of the dry season, enabling the breakthrough
to be constructed in the dry.
13.         The present design includes underground trashracks and gates
located in shn.Lts remote from the intake, in order to minimize the surface
construction which must be carried out in a relatively short period. However,
GOMID are currently studying the possibility of extending the lake drawdown



-96-
Annex 3.1
Page 4 of 11
period, such that the trashracks could be located at the actual intake. This
would considerably simplify the underground excavations in this area.
14.         A 9.1 m x 9.1 m D-shaped concrete lined headrace tunnel 4.5 km
long terminates in a restricted orifice tvpe surge shaft, 21.8 m in diameter
and 110 m high. The maximum capacity of the tunnel is about 230 m./s. From
the base of the shaft four 3.9 m steel lined tunnels lead to butterfly valves
housed in a cavern located at the junction with the high pressure shafts.
15.         The four steel lined high pressure shafts are each 3.9 m in
diameter and 530 m long, inclined at an angle of 60 degrees to the horizontal.
These terminate in short horizontal lengths of penstock leading to the 2.4 m
diameter spherical valves located within the powerhouse cavern.
16.          The powerhouse cavern, which has a span of 22 m, a height of 48 m
and a length of 143 m houses four Francis type turbine generator units each
rated at 250 MW under 485 m head. The thirteen single phase 15/400 kV
transformers, each rated at 100 MVA are housed in a separate cavern which has
a span of 22 m and a length of 158 m.  The transformers are connected to a
conventional outdoor switchyard by 400 kV oil filled cables located in a
separate tunnel. A separate cavern is also provided for the collection
manifold and draft tube gate and a number of smaller tunnels are provided for
access, ventilation, etc. Access to the powerh-use is through a double lane
tunnel about 2 km long.
17.         A single 10 m x 10 m tailrace tunnel 2150 m long is provided. This
is locally enlarged at its upstream end to provide for tailrace surge. The
tailrace tunnel discharges into a 165 m length of open channel which
terminates in a gated outlet structure discharging into the forebay of the
existing Koyna Stage III development.
Koyna Dam
18.         Koyna Dam is an essential component of the Koyna IV powerplant and
consideration must therefore be given to its adequacy to function throughout
the desigr. life of the development. It is a rubble masonry gravity dam witlh a
maximum height above foundation level of 85 m.
19.         The dam suffered damage during the strong earthquake of December
11, 1967 which devastated the surrounding area and resulted in the loss of
almost 200 lives. Thp epicenter of the tremor was calculated to be very close
to the damsite and its magnitude was in the order of 6.5. Strong vibrations
were experienced throughout the structure with a peak acceleration of 0.63g
being recorded on a strong motion accelerograph located within the dam. As a
result some cracking occurred in the dam although the more significant damage
related to appurtenant structures. Seismic activity of a more minor nature
has continued since the major tremor.
20.         Remedial measures immediately following the earthquake included
epoxy grouting of visible cracks, drilling of internal drainage holes and



-97-
Aimex 3, 1
Page 5 of 11
strengther.ing of seven high monoliths with vertical prestressed cables
anchored well into the body of thte dam. Subsequently, buttresses were
consttucted downstream of the non-overflow monoliths with a construction
procedure aimed at achieving a full structural connection between the new and
old constructions. The strengthened section was designed on the basis of
pseudo-static methods, but in recent years the desigr has been checked using
the results of a dynamic analysis carried out by Roorkee University. The dam
is well instrumented and instrumentation has been continuously monitored over
the years. Results have been periodically analysed and reviewed by a panel of
experts which has met at regular intervals since the time of the major tremor.
With the measures that have been taken to restore, strengthen and re-analyze
the dam, the design complies with current requirements appropriate to a highly
seismic area. However, the Bank has recommended some limited additional
investigations aimed at proving certain design assumptions. GOMID has agreed
to carry these out as part of the rehabilitation measures described below.
21.         Leakage through the dam has been increasing since the time of
construction. The vast majority is through contraction joints between the
blocks indicating failure of the copper waterstops either at .oints between
sections or joints with the concrete. As might be expected, leakage increased
sharply following the major tremor, which could be attributed to differential
vibrations between adjacent monoliths exacerbating the progressive failure
which was already occurring. Later increases in the rate appear to be
unrelated to seismic activity.
22.         Structurally, the quantity of leakage has no significance provided
the provisions to evacuate it are adequate as they are in the case of Koyna.
Moreover, the current rate of leakage is not unusually high for a dam of this
type. However it has increased by a factor of 10 over the life of the dam and
if it continued to increase at the current rate for another ten or twenty
years it would eventually become unacceptable even if only from economic or
aesthetic standpoints. Over the years GOMID has made several attempts to seal
the joints without significant success. To a large extent, failures are
attributed to the fact that repairs have been confined to areas above Minimum
Operating Level of the reservoir, which can be accessed in the dry. GOMID has
therefore agreed to the employment of a specialists to carry out necessary
repair works including underwater work as required. The work would be
financed by the Bank as part of the project.
Koyna Reservoir
23.         Koyna reservoir has a gross storage of 2797 hm3 and a live storage
of 2685 hm3. Siltation surveys have been carried out since filling of the
reservoir, the most recent in 1985. These have indicated a relatively low
rate of storage depletion amounting to about one percent since filling. The
implication is that live storage will not be significantly reduced within the
life of the project.



-98-
Annex 3.1
Page 6 of 11
Power and Energy
24.         The Koyna IV station would add 1000 MW of peaking capacity to the
interconnected system. As noted earlier, the quantity of flow will not be
increased and th'Krefore total energy output of the Koyna complex would not
vary substantially. However a minor addition to energy output would be gained
because of the increased hydraulic efficiency of the new Francis units in
comparison with Peltons and also because they can utilize slightly more of the
available head. The increased energy amounts to 47 GWh bringing total energy
output to 2197 GWh. Both power and energy are firm, that is they can be
utilized at a constant rate throughout the year, and will be fully available
in at least 95 percent of years.
25.         Stages I, II and IV would operate together to provide a total of
1560 MW of peaking power to the grid at a plant load factor of 17 percent.
The lower Stage III development is already designed as a peaking station with
a plant load factor of about 17.5 percent after 1994. Upper and lower
developments can be operated independently and at different load factors on a
daily basis because of the balancing reservoir between the two developments.

under construction. In addition GOMID intends to install 123 MW of Small
Canal Head hydro plants, which would be handed for operation upon completion
to MSEB.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Terms of Reference for Panel of Exnerts
for the Koyna IV Hydroelectric Power Plant
1.          The main purpose of the Panel is the assurance of safe and
economic designs and efficient construction methods. This will be achieved
through periodic reviews during the detailed engineering and construction
phases to assess the need for changes in the design or in the construction
methods.
2.          The panel will include permanent members, expert in the following
fields:
- Rock Mechanics and Geotechnical Engineering and Underground Works
- Construction of Underground Works
- Concrete Dams and Hydraulic Structure Design
- Mechanical and Electrical including Large Size Turbines and
Generators.
3.          In discharging its duties the Panel may, if it co.nsiders it to be
advisable, request the assistance on a temporary basis, of experts in
specialized fields to extend the range of expertise of the Panel.
4.          The Panel, unless they otherwise decide, will meet regularly every
four months to review the status of technical works. It will also meet at the
request of the project authorities, if the need for more immediate
consultation were to arise.
5.          The Panel will prepare and submit written proceedings of its
meetings (regular or extraordinary) including recommendations, within three
weeks of each meeting, and in cases of urgency, will prepare technical
memorandums or aide memoire immediately after or during the meetings. A
preliminary report will be prepared before the adjournment of each meeting.
The preliminary reports, proceedings and any other memorandums, aide memoire
etc. will be submitted to the Project authorities and through them to the
Bank.
6.          The panel's responsibilities shall include but not necessarily be
limited to reviews of, and recommendation of necessary changes:
(a)   Layout modifications within the overall concept established in the
bid drawings;



-119-
Annex 3.7
Page 2 of 2
(b)   Design of individual components of the project including design
criteria;
(c)   The field investigation program covering geological and
geotechnical investigations in relation to surface and underground
structures and construction materials;
(d)   Concrete mix design, concrete material processing, testing and
quality control of concrete materials and concrete;
(e)   Design of temporary and permanent excavation supports for surface
and underground excavations;
(f)   Construction procedures, particularly in relation to underground
excavation and concreting;
(g)   Technical specification for major civil works and equipment;
(h)   Construction scheduling and planning; and
(i)   Instrumentation of underground excavations and of Koyna dam.
7.          The Panels assignment will extend until the completion of
construction of the project.

Annex 3.8

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Suggested Terms of Reference for Engineering Consultancy Services
for Koyna IV
1.          The Maharashtra Irrigation Department is responsible for the
implementation of the Koyna IV Hydroelectric Plant. The design of the plant
to bidding level and the preparation of the draft bid documents has been
completed by the technical staff of the Department, and its construction
forces are engaged in preliminary works associated with construction. During
the implementation of the project the Department's design and construction
personnel will continue their heavy involvement, including the assumption of
direct responsibility for the following works:
(a) calling of bids and award of contracts;
(b) preparation of detailed designs for civil works, station
auxiliaries, gates, infrastructure and other miscellaneous works;
(c) the supervision of equipment supply contracts including checking
of manufacturers designs and drawings for major mechanical and
electrical equipment;
(d) the construction, using its own forces, of a component of the
civil works for the project which includes access roads and
tunnels, tailrace tunnel and infrastructure works;
(e) supervision of three major contracts for the bulk of the civil
works; and
(f) erection and installation under manufacturer's supervision of the
permanent plant and equipment in the powerhouse and switchyard.
2.          The project layout has been generally finalized and approved by
the Panel of Consultants (POE) that has been constituted for this project.
Therefore no further studies are anticipated in relation to project concept.
However the layout of individual structures may b-e reviewed during detailed
design with a view to obtaining maximum efficiency and economy.
3.          The Department's technical personnel have substantial experience
in the type of works envisaged. However in view of the importance of the
project, the Department intends to employ consultants to provide specialist
advice and assistance in specific areas with the object of achieving state of
the art design solutions and of maximizing the efficiency and economy of the
construction process. Certain areas where such assistance will be requested
have been identified at this stage, but the Department wishes to retain the



-121-
Page 2 of 3
option of requesting specialist assistance in other areas throughout the
course of the project, as and when the need for such assistance is identified.
4.    The specific areas, which have been identified at this stage, where the
advice and assistance of consultants will likely be required, are set out int
he following paragraphs. Department staff will have the primary
responsibility for carrying out the work, with consultants operating in an
advisory role.
(a)   technical evaluation of ICB civil works bids in accordance with
Bank procurement procedures;
(b)   technical evaluation of bids for turbines and generators;
(c) review of manufacturers designs ,drawings and model testing for
turbines and generators;
(d)   supervision of erection of turbine and generator units, and
testing and commissioning of the installation;
(e)   training in the use of computer methods in project management,
including supply of software for spreadsheet analysis and project
management, and microcomputers and associated hardware necessary
for utilization of the programs.
5.          Advice may be requested in any other area relating to the
Department's participation in this project. Therefore consultants should have
wide experience in the design and supervision of construction of large
underground works and in particular should have experience in the following
areas:
(a)   design and supervision of underground works of comparable scale to
those envisaged involving modern design methods and construction
techniques appropriate to Indian conditions;
(b)   design of permanent excavation support systems for underground
structures including specification of design imposed constraints
on construction procedures, and associated geotechnical and rock
mechanics analyses; if required by the Department this may incluide
review of design provisions in the light of experience in the
early stages of construction;
(c)   preparation of specifications and supervision of supply, erection
and testing of high head Francis turbines, and generators of
comparable size to those planned;
(d)   management of large projects involving international bidding and
utilizing modern construction planning techniques.



-122-
Annex 3.8
Page 3 of 3
6.          The consultants will nominate in their proposals specialists in
the fields covered by para 4 above. One of the specialists will be nominated
as a coordinator of consultant input over the course of the project
continuously liaising with the Department and advising where specialist
assistance will be required. It is not anticipated that the assignment of the
coordinator would need to be full time over the course of the project, but
extended assignments might be expected during critical stages. The timing and
duration of the assignments of specialists would be related to the execution
of particular project activities. The consultants input would be expected to
be more intense during the early stages of the project and during the early
stages of construction of critical elements.
7.          Specialists in other areas will be deployed at the request of, or
as approved, by the Department.
8.          Although the large majority of the services under this contract
are expected to be provided in India, specialized services may be provided
from headquarters offices, where it is more efficient to do so, for example
where laboratory or computer facilities are not locally available, and
considered to be necessary by the Department.
9.    The scope of work of the consultant will include training of the
Department's personnel in state of the art techniques in the work areas
covered by para 4. The consultants should detail in their proposal
recommended methods for achieving the required transfer of technology. These
may include, in addition to the "on the job" training associated with the
current assignment, secondment of Department staff to design offices and work
sites, visits to work sites and manufacturing facilities, and formal training
where appropriate.
10.         The overall consultant months expected to be required for the
services including miscellaneous advice covered by para 7 above is about 100.
The actual input would however be dependant on the requirements of the
Department as determined during the course of the project.
11.         Taking into account the objectives outlined above, the consultants
shall prepare proposals, detailing their methodology and approach to the
assignment, together with assignment schedules and consultant month estimates
relating to the specific aspects where advice and assistance will be required
as identified above.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT'
Suggested Terms of Reference for Consultancy Services for Dam Repair Work
1.          The Maharashtra Irrigation Department is currently constructing
the Koyna IV hydroelectric power plant which is supplied from the reservoir of
the existing Koyna Dam. Since the dam is an essential component of all stages
of the Koyna development, the Department intends to carry out repair works to
it, to ensure a design life consistent with that of the newly constructed
elements of the development.
2.         The Koyna Dam, which was completed in 1961 is of rubble masonry
construction and has a maximum height above foundation level of 85 m. The dam
suffered damage during the strong earthquake of December 11, 1967 which had a
magnitude of about 6.5.  Strong vibrations were experienced  throughout the
structure resulting in some cracking. Seismic activity of a more minor nature
has continued since the major tremor. Remedial measures immediately following
the earthquake included epoxy grouting of visible cracks, drilling of internal
drainage holes and strengthening of seven high monoliths with vertical
prestressed cables anchored well into the body of the dam. Subsequently,
buttresses were constructed downstream of the non- overflow monoliths with a
construction procedure aimed at achieving a full structural connection between
the new and old constructions. Monitoring of instrumentation since the
original tremor, and static and dynamic analyses of the revised section
indicate no cause for concern from the structural viewpoint. Howevor the rate
of leakage through the dam which increased sharply at the time of the
earthquake has continued to increase since that time, with identifiable
discrete increases in rate apparently unrelated to seismic activity.
Currently the total leakage rate through the dam is about 70 l/s. The great
majority of leakage flows from into the ups-ream drainage gallery through
block joints and is generally considered to result from failure of copper
waterstops at joints between sections or with the surrounding concrete.
Several attempts to seal the block joints have been made with no significant
success, possibly because work on the upstream face has been confined to that
area above minimum operating level of the reservoir.
3.          The Department intends to employ consultants highly specialists in
repair of concrete dams especially in the area of underwater treatment to
carry out the following work:
Consultancy services would be retained following recommendation of
the DRP. The panel may amend this proposal upon completion of its
review (para 3.08).



-124-
Page 2 of 2
(a)   A thorough review of the existing records of leakage and other
instrumentation together with previous compilations and analyses
from tChxem, leading to the determination of requirements for
further investigations. These further investigations are expected
to include comprehensive underwater inspections of the upstream
face to allow the compilation of a complete inventory of cracks
and sources of inflow into and through the dam.
(b)   Preparation of specifications for further investigations.  The
Department is equipped to carry out investigations of a non-
specialized nature including diamond drilling, water pressure
testing etc. and may elect to carry out this work using its own
resources.
(c)   Supervision of additional investigations, compilation of results
and preparation of a report detailing recommendations for repair
and providing cost estimates for same. After agreement of the
Department on the scope of repair works, the consultant shall be
responsible for the preparation of bid documents for repair works,
calling of international competitive bids and evaluation of the
bids, award of the contract and supervision of the repair works.
4.          Investigations since the major earthquake have determined that the
dam is structurally adequate in accordance with modern earthquake design
criteria. Consequently, the emphasis of required consultancy services has
been placed on minimization of leakage by treatment of the dam below water
level. However, the consultants should be mindful of structural implications
when reviewing investigation results and notify the Department of any result
which may have adverse structural implications. Where appropriate the
Consultant may suggest, or the Department may request, additional
investigations aimed at confirming structural adequacy.
5.          The Department will provide the Consultant with full access to
instrumentation results and other data relating to the proposed services.

INDI
&HO&RASHTRA POWER PROJECT
proposed Terms of Regerence for Studies of Load Dispatch
and Interstate Power Trading in the Western Region
Objective
1.       The overall objectives of the program of work described below are
to provide WREB and its members with: (i) regularly updated information on
the costs of operating generating plant in the Western Region; and (ii) the
data, models and other facilities to compute optimal patterns of load
dispatch for the Western Region for a variety of load and system
conditions, having regard to technical and contractual constraints on
interstate power exchanges. These schedules will provide WREB and its
members with a valuable tool for identifying further opportunities for
economic load dispatch, particularly during off-peak periods. A third
objective of the work program is to review present commercial arrangements
for interstate power exchanges. To meet these three objectives the work
program will comprise five main tasks, each of which is described below.
(a) DeveloR two models describing the marginal generating costs of
thermal and hydroelectric power stations. These models will
provide cost inputs to the generation scheduling model (c). The
model for thermal stations will describe the marginal costs of
each unit in terms of heat rate and coal quality, the costs of
coal and oil co.;sumed and marginal maintenance ^osts. The model
would be capable of quoting costs for incremental and decremental
generation from several different load conditions.  The model for
hydro stations would provide similar cost data on the basis of
marginal maintenance costs and the replacement value of firm
energy.
(b) Research the economic and additional technical data needed. This
task will provide data for the two generation cost models. WREB
already has much of the technical data needed, though not all of
it is collected centrally. A fair amount of work will be required
both to complete data collection and to relate technical and
economic data for individual plants.  For example, the models for
each generating set will need to include relationships between
coal quality, oil burn and plant efficiency. Similarly, data are
needed on off-load heat costs and on the division of maintenance
costs between fixed and variable components.
(c)  Develop a model to assist WREB in planning station disRatch.  At
present station dispatch is planned manually. The proposed model
would ease this computational burden and highlight opportunities
for economic load dispatch, particularly during off-peak periods.
The model would also enable scheduling to be undertaken more
frequently than currently possible. Thus, load dispatch will be



-126-                        Annex 3.10
Page 2 of 2
able to follow actual load conditions more closely and to react to
unforeseen occurrences (such ac a loss of generation) in the most
economic way. The model would use data on station marginal costs
from the models developed in (a) and would also make proper
allowances for physical and contractual constraints to economic
load dispatch.
(d)  Install systems to update the data bases of the generation costing
and station scheduling models. For the generation and station
scheduling models to be most useful to WREB, they will need to be
regulariy updated, probably more regularly than WREB presently
collects the technical and economic data involved. Existing data
collection arrangements therefore are likely to need strengthening
to increase the frequency of data collection and to arrange data
in ways that are needed for the models.
(e) Review SEBs' arrangements for exchanges of shares of NTPC and
NHPC generation. Currently, SEBs' exchange generation shares at
the average of the prices which they have paid to NTPC and NHPC
for the surplus generation which they deposit in the regional                  k
pool. With improved information on marginal generation costs,
SEBs will have an opportunity to adjust the commercial basis on
which they exchange central generation to reflect marginal
generation costs more closely. This will make trading more
equitable amongst SEBs. The review would recommend adjustments to
existing commercial arrangements for power exchanges that would
improve the financial and economic efficiency of the regional
power market.



-127-
Annex 3.11
INDIA
NAHARASHTA POWER PROJECT
Details to be included in Annual Resettlement and
Rehabilitation Plans
The following is the information required in regard to
resettlement as part of the annual work program to be furnished by MSEB to
the Bank (paras 3.12 and 3.17):
(i) Amount in ha of private land and government land to be acquired;
(ii)  Number of houses or other structures occupying the land to be
acquired, and the range of sizes and types;
(iii) Number of landowning families/persons affected by acquisition of
houses and/or land;
(iv)  Number of renter families/persons affected by acquisition of
houses and/or land;
(v) Number of squatter families/persons affected by acquisition of
houses and/or land;
(vi)  Socioeconomic profile of the project-affected families (types of
occupations, range of incomes, ethnic or religious
characteristics, educational levels, average family size,
presence/absence of livestock, etc);
(vii) Amount in ha of private land or government land to be allotted to
project-affected families for houseplots;
(viii) Public services to be replaced and provided to the newly allotted
houseplots;
(ix)  Other programs, as may be required, to enable the project-affected
families to rebuild and reestablish their production/employment
activities (i.e. building materials, transport, hauling,
resettlement allowance for subsistence while rebuilding, etc.);
and
(x) Cost estimates for period of the above disaggregated into project
land acquisition, replacement land acquisition, site development
for replacement land, resettlement/transfer activities and
rehabilitation/rebuilding activities.

-134-                      Annex 4.4
Page 1 of 2
INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Financial Action Plan
1. Objectives
The objectiveq of the Financial Action P'lan aret
(1) To allocate the financial burden of HSEB's future capital
investment cost in a balanced manner between Consumers through future
electricity tariff adjustments, Maharashtra State Government through future
GOM loans, and MSEB through increased efforts to reduce costs and improve
productivity.
(2) To further improve MSEB's long-term financial viability
defined as its capability to meet its financial obligations from own
resources.
(3) For MSEB to contribute to resolve the emerging arrears issue
in the power sector.
2. Actions
MSEB will implement the following actions:
(1) MSEB will control working expenditures including personnel
expenditures and future recruitment, especially in the Distribution sector,
so that the total labour force increase will be no more than an average 2Z
per annum over the FY 1989190 to 1994/95 period. The labour force increase
should be justified.
(2) To improve MSEB's weak capital structure, GOM and MSEB will
furnish to the Bank by March 31, 1990, alternative financing terms for GOM
loans.
(3) GOM and MSEB will revise, by December 31, 1989, and furnish to
the Bank for comment the mode of calculation of the lease-rent for new
hydro power plants and revise it as necessary.
(4) MSEB will coordinate annual revisions to its five-year
investment plan with its global corporate financing plan, and ensure that
long-term financing requirements for capital investment and working capital
financing are consistent with MSEB's capacity to generate funds, and with
the long-term loans available, including GOM loans.  Coordination will be
carried out prior to December 31 of each year for the next five financial
years.
(5) MSEB will reduce payment delays to suppliers of fuel, power
and consumables, and expedite reconciliation procedures for disputed
amounts.



-135-
Annex 4.4
Page 2 of 2
S. Performmce Indicators
The timely implementation of the actions listed in para 2 above,
and any additional action deemed necessary by HSEB's management will help
MSEB achieve the following financial performance targets, which are part of
the Financial Action Plan.

Income LZatementst
REWVEUS
1.       Averaae Tariff.  A 52 adjustment in PY 1988/89, in accordance with
the most recent information. After PY 1988/89, required adjustments were
estimated as follows (i) a targetted average tariff was calculated, based
on projected generation, operating costs, depreciation, financial charges
and interest, and the necessary net income to achieve the targetted rate of
return and (ii) tariffs were adjusted for each category of consumer
separately, so that tariff structure is gradually improved, and the weighted
average of tariff increases for each category remains in line with the the
targetted average tariff.
2.       Other Revenues.  Includes meter rentals and revenues from other
sources than sales of electricity. It is projected to grow at the same pace
as sales of electricity.
3.       Electricity Duty.  Corresponds to an average tax of Ps. 4 per kWh
(actual level in FY 1987/88). Projected to increase in line with energy
sales in kWh.
4.       Subsidies and Grants.  Calculated as the amount necessary for MSEB
to earn a 9.5S return before interest on its capital base, defined as
average net fixed assets and estimated working capital required (estimated
arbitrarily as 1/6 of working expenses), financed from other resources than
equity or consumer deposits. The mode of calculation was agreed between
MSEB and GOM, and is covenanted under Bank Rural Electrification Projects.
5.        Other Income.  Includes interest received, and revenues from
miscellaneous transaction. It is projected to grow in line with local
inflation.



-137-                       Annex 4.5
Page 2 of 4
6.  Works on Force Account.  Represents the value capital investment
implemented by MSEB's own staff. It includes mainly investment in
distribution and sub-stations. It is projected to increase in line -ith
generation and local inflation. Corresponding consumption of production
factors is included in working expenses.
"PZNS'S
7.       Generation of Power.  Includes generation costs other thar. fuel
cost, lease rent, personnel and maintenance. Projected to increase in line
with generation and inflation.
8.       Fuel Cost.  includes coal, fuel oil, and natural gas cost for
thermal stations. Calculated on the basis of the generation plan for each
plant, and effective FY 1987/88 fuel consumption. For new plants,
consumption is based on engineering estimates. Future fuel cost estimates
per kWh for thermal generation at constant 1988 prices are as follows:
1988/89     30.95 Ps.      1992/93       30.68 Ps.
1989/90     30.21 Ps       1993/94       30.62 Ps.
1990/91     29.49 Ps.      1994/95       30.90 Ps.
1991/92     29.24 Ps.      1995/96       30.90 Ps.
9.       Lease Rent.  The lease rent is the rent paid by HSEB to GOMID for
operations of the hydro plants owned by GOHID. It is calculated on the
basis of a constant annuity equivalent to the construction cost at an 82
interest over 30 years.
10.      Purchase of Power.  Calculated on the basis of purchases from Tata,
NTPC, other States, and Atomic Energy Commission, at the present price for
each of these sources, escalated in line with local inflation. The average
cost of energy purchased at 1987/88 prices is as followst
1988/89     67 Ps.         1992/93     56 Ps.
1989/90     59 Ps          1993/94     56 Ps.
1990/91     57 Ps.         1994/95     56 Ps.
1991/92     57 Ps.         1995/96     56 Ps.
11.      Repair and Maintenance.  Estimated as .25 Rs. per million of gross
fixed assets in use.
12.      Employee Cost.  Includes all fringe benefits.  It is assumed that
the result of the Manpower Planning exercise will be that labor force will
increase by only 2.? p.a. over the next six years. Labor unit cost is
projected to increase in line with local inflation.
13.      General Administration.  Includes insurance, rents, transportation
and comm_mications. Projected co increase in line with local inflation.
14.      Other Expenses.  Includes interest on consumer deposits, etc.  It
is projected to increase in line with local inflation.
15.      Depreciation.  Depreciation is calculated in accordance with the
Electricity (Supply) Act on a linear basis, at a rate of 3.5? per annum.



-138-
Annex 4.5
Page 3 of 4
16.      Interest for the Year.  Includes interest on long term loans
calculated as follows for new loanst
PPC                    13?             8 Years, 1 grace
NABAD                   10           12 Years, 1 grace
LIC                     15Z           14 Years
Market Bonds         11.5S            15 Years maturity
REC                    11?            Variable
MSCB                    142            5 Years, 1 grace
UTI                     142            7 Years, 3 grace
For GOM loans, interest is 9?, with a moratorium on interest and principal
repayment of 6 years, while cumulative interest is accruing on deferred
interest and principal at a 9? rate and charged to operations. Repayment
period is 30 years, of which 6 years of moratorium. Interest for the year
are interest accru-d, and do not correspond to interest actually paid.
17.      Interest During Construction.  Includes the percentage of interest
accrued (but not necessarily payable) to be added to Works in Progress, and
to be capitalized when work is completed. The percentage is calculated in
accordance with the Electricity (Supply) Act provision, approximately as
the ratio of accumulated Works in Progress to Gross Fixed Assets financed
from other sources than Equity or Consumer Deposits. Projected Interest
During Construction are calculated based on MSEB's investment plan
(Annex 4.7), and investment financing plan (Annex 4.8).
18.      Interest for Previous Years.  Includes interest on GOM loans
payable, but not paid because of insufficient surplus, and deferred to the
next year. This was the case in FY 1982/83 and FY 1983/84, but is not
projected to recur in the future.
19.      Deferred Expenses.  Under the pre-1985 accounting system,
depreciation could be deferred whenever net result was insufficient. This
practice has been discontinued under the 1985 accounting system.
BALANCE SHET
20.      Capital Works in Progress.  Calculated as the balance between
additions to VIP for the year based on estimated project implementation
schedule, and transfer to Fixed Assets at the end of the fiscal year of
completed projects.
21.      Accounts Payable on Investment.  Projected as 0.5 months of
expenditures for the year on capital investment excluding Works on Force
Account. In the past, the corresponding figure was from 0.2 to 0.7 months.
22.      Accounts Payable on Operations.  Projected as 0.7 months of cash
operating expenses. FY 1987/88 level is 0.9 months. Reduction is
justified on the basis of expected improvement in MSEB's cash situation.
23.      Consumers Contributions.  Includes consumers guarantee deposits
and connection charges. Projected to remain at present level of 2.6 months
of sales of electricity (1.6 months for connection charges, and 1 month for
guarantee deposit).



-139-                      Annex 4.5
Page 4 of 4
24.      Other Short Term Liabilities.  Projected to increase in line with
local inflation.
25.      Fuel Stores.  Projected to remain at the present level of 3.3
months of fuel consumption.
26.      Capital Stores.  Projected to remain at the present level of 1
month of Repair and Maintenance expenditures.
27.      Sundry Debtors.  Projected to increase in line with local
inflation.
28.      Advances to Staff.  Projected to remain at the present level of
0.6 months of the wage bill.
29.      Commercial Receivables.  Commercial Receivables have been
increasing in the past from 2.2 months of sales in FY 1982/83 to 3.7 months
of sales in FY 1987/88, mainly because of the increase in Receivables from
Other States. MSEB has included in its financial targets the reduction of
commercial receivables to 2.5 months of sales by FY 1990/91.
30.      Other Receivables.  Projected to increase in line with local
inflation.
31.      Long Term Loans.  New long term loans were calculated as follows:
PFC                        300 millions annually
NABAD                      150 millions annually
LIC                        +15? over previous year's borruwings;
200 millions in 1989
Bonds                     +10? over previous year's borrowings,
plus repayment
REC Plan                   +10? over previous year's borrowings;
100 millions in 1989
REC Non-Plan               400 millions annually
REC Associated Banks       800 millions annually
Other Loans, IDBI          +102 over previous year's borrowings;
150 millions in 1989
Other +lOZ over previous year's
borrowings; 500 millions in 1989
GOM                        Balance between Investment Program for
the year at 1989 prices estimated
working capital requirement, and
MSEB's cash flow sfter debt service,
plus Plan loans received during the
year.

INDIA
MAHARASHTRA POWER PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.       India's power systems are organized into five regional networks:
Western, Eastern, Southern, Northern and North-Eastern. To capture
available economies of scale and to benefit from diversity in the timing of
states' peak demands, CEA plans power systems development at the regional
level. Because of this, it is appropriate to focus economic analysis on
the Western Region power system where the proposed project is located.
This regional network comprises the States of Maharashtra, Hadhya Pradesh
and Gujarat, and the Union Territories of Goa, Daman & Diu, and Dadra E
Nagar Haveli.
Electricity Demand in the Western Region
2.       The consumption of electricity in the Western Region over the
period 1981/82 through 1987/88, together with projected demands through
1999/2000 are summarized in Table 1. For the Region as a whole, the
maximum load met increased from 5,811 MW in 1981182 to 11,060 MW in
1987/88; growth averaging 11.4Z per annum. The amount of energy supplied
increased from 30,378 GWh to 62,082 GWh over the same period; growth
averaging 12.7Z per annum. Within the Region. Table 1 shows that maximum
load and energy supplied grew most quickly in Madhya Pradesh (13.92 and
15.6Z per annum respectively) and most slowly in Goa (6.1S and 7.7Z per
annum respectively). Maximum load in Haharashtra grew at 10.41 per annum
and energy supplied at 10.1 per annum. Industrial consumers account for
just under 601 of total energy consumption ir. the Region and agricultural
consumers for about 161 of consumption (Table 2). Domestic consumers
account for a further 14X and commercial consumers for an additional 61.
Comparing 1981/82 with 1987188, consumption growth has been strongest
amongst agricultural and domestic consumers (12.81 and 12.41 per annum
respectively). Industrial consumption grew at only 7.2? per annum and
commercial consumption at 8.11 per annum.
3.       CRA projects that by 199912000, total energy demand in the Western
Region will have increased to 163,328 GWh and the maximum power demand will
have increased to 31,987 MW (Table 1). Compared with the estimated demand
in 1988189 of 67,181 GWh and 12,956 MW, the forecast implies growth in
energy demand averaging 8.41 per annum and maximum load growth averaging
8.61 per annum. 1/ Total electricity consumption is forecast to increase
from 51,404 GWh to 129,865 GWh over the same period: growth averaging 8.81
per annum. 2/ Growth in consumption is expected to be lead by domestic
1/ The difference between demand and consumption reflects supply
constraints and technical losses.
2/ The difference between demand and consumption reflects supply
constraints and techr4cal losses.



-148-
Annex 5.1
Page 2 of 12
consumers (13.02 per annum), while demand growth in agriculture is expected
to ease to 8.61 per annum (Table 2).  Industrial demand is expected to
increase at 7.42 per annum and commercial demand at 7.7Z per annum. The
expected changes in the structure of demand growth will change sectoral
shares in consumption. By 1999/2000, the share of industry in total
consumption is expected to have declined to just under 5OZ, while the share
of domestic consumption is expected to have increased to nearly 22Z. The
share of agriculture Is expected to remain about 162, while that of
commerce is expected to decline to just under 52 of consumption (Table 2).
Even though generating capacity is projected to increase at 10.1? per
annum, Table 1 suggests that in 1995/96 (when Koyna IV would be
commissioned) the Western Region vill still face a shortage of generating
capacity of about 162.
4.       Between 1981/82 and 1985/86, India's elasticity of electricity
consumption to economic growth was approximately 1.6. In the Western
Region, this elasticity was approximately 2.1, reflecting the high share of
industry in the Region-: economic activity. Some reduction of th2 Region's
high elasticity is likely in view of changing sectoral patterns of
consumption (Table 2) and with the prospect of some improvement in end-ti1.2'
efficiencies. 3/ Even so, compared with GOI's targets for economic growth
of 6X per annum under the Eighth Plan and growth of about 5X per annum
thereafter, projected increases in electricity demand in the Western Region
of 8.4? per annum (Table 1) appear conservative. This suggests, therefore,
that there is little possibility that the additional capacity to be
provided in the Western Rsgion development program through 1995/96 will not
be utilized fully upon commissioning. The project analysis reported below
suggests further that, in the event that some adjustment of the program is
required, deferral of other projects would provide greater economies than
deferral of Koyna IV. Hence, there is little possibility that the
additional capacity to be provided through the Koyna IV project will not be
utilised fully upon commissioning.
Least-Cost Analysis
5.       CEA's planning of least-cost generation development in the Western
Region shows that extension of the existing Koyna hydroelectric station is
part of the least-cost plan. CEA's analyses also show that: (i) the
optimum installed capacity for Koyna IV is 1,000 MW; and (ii) benefits
would be maximized through the earliest feasible commissioning of the
project. CEA's techniques of analysis and the assumptions it has used
(including its projections of electricity demand) have been reviewed by the
Bank. In some important respects, CEA's planning is over-simplified, for
example planning is undertaken in financial rather than economic terms.
Because of this, CEA and the Bank have undertaken additional studies to
review: (i) the justification for the inclusion of Koyna IV in the least-
cost program; and (ii) the optimization of the station's capacity and
proposed date of commissioning. These studies are summarized below.
3/ Improvements are likely to be only modest as many markets lock
competitive pressures to reduce costs.



-149-
Annex 5.1
Page 3 of 12
Identifying the Least-Cost Program
6.       The present value of capital and operating costs of the Western
Region development program through 1997/98 including Koyna IV has been
compared (in economic terms) with the costs of programs including the
cheapest alternatives to the project. Three alternatives have been
considered, each specified so as to provide approximately the same peak
generating capacity as Koyna IV. 4/ Comparisons have been taken through
1997/98 to accommodate the implementation schedules of feasible
alternatives to Koyna IV. Comparisons are based on avoidable costs, that
is the costs associated with new projects, as the costs of projects under
implementation are common to each of the alternatives. Table 3 summarizes
results and shows that the program including Koyna IV offers economies of
approximately Rs 6,445 million (US$430 million) compared with programs
including the cheapest alternative projects. 5/ For Koyna IV to loose its
advantage over the cheapest alternative program, the costs of the project
would have to more than double, or system fuel costs would have to decrease
by 272 in real terms. Both these eventualities are considered very
unlikely. Hence the inclusion of Koyna IV in the least-cost generation
program is likely to be justified.
Optimizing Project Options
7.       CEA's optimization of the installed capacity of Koyna IV has been
reviewed by estimating the present value of total system costs (again in
economic terms) for projects with installed capacities of 500 KW, 750 MW
and 1000 MW. No larger options have been considered as the station's
generation then would be provided at too low a load factor for it to be
easily absorbed within the Western Region system. Comparability between
the three project alternatives has been ensured through adjusting the
background investment programs so that each has approximately the same peak
generating capacity. 6/ Table 4 gives the present value of total system
costs for the three capacity options and shows that the program including
Koyna IV with 100n MW installed is the cheapest option.  For a 750 MW
installation to be cheaper, system fuel costs would have to decrease by
about 7.9Z in real terms, or the capital costs of thermal plant relative to
hydro plant would have to decrease by about 8.2Z. Both eventualities are
considered very unlikely. This supports CEA's optimization of Koyna IV's
capacity at 1,000 MW.
41 Comparisons have allowed for the higher energy generation of thermal
alternatives to Koyna IV. As the Western Region is predominantly short
of power rather than energy (Table 1), the benefits of thic additional
generation accrue mostly in the form of fuel cost savings from
displacing more costly plant from operation.
5/ Full details of there comparisons, and the sensitivity analysis
reported in following paragraphs, are available in the Project File.
6/ Allowance has again been made for the higher energy generation of
supplementary thermal capacity.

Annex 5.1
Page 4 of 12
8.       CEA's optimization of the proposed date of commissioning of
Koyna IV has been reviewed through estimating the effect on the present
value of total system costs of delaying the station's commissioning (and
the start of construction) by one, two and three years.  To compensate for
these delays, the commissioning of other plant in the Western Reqion
program has been advanced correspondingly. Table 5 presents resuits and
shows that the earliest feasible commissioning of Koyna IV minimizes the
present value of total system costs. For a one-year delay in commissioning
Koyna IV to be economic, the fuel costs of thermal plant would have to
decline by about 4.6Z in real terms, or the capital costs of thermal plant
relative to hydro plant would have to decline by about 4.7Z. These
eventualities are again considered very unlikely; hence the proposed
commissioning date of 1994/96 is likely to be optimal.
Program and Project Analyses
9.       Benefits that will be derived from the components of the proposed
project cannot easily be separated from benefits that will be derived from
other investments in generation, transmission and distribution in the
Western Region. Because of this, it is appropriate to focus cost-benefit
analysis primarily on the Western Region development program as a whole,
having first established that the planned program is the least-cost option
available (para 6). For this purpose, analysis has focussed on the 1989/90
to 1996/97 time-slice of investments in the development program. This
period covers implementation and the first two years of operation of Koyna
IV and implementation of most other new generation projects that will be
started in the Western Region over the next couple of years. This analysis
is reported below.
10.      When the average level of retail power prices is below marginal
cost (as in India), it does not follow automatically that the selection of
a project into a least-cost program justifies the project in question as
economic, even if the program as a whole is shown to be economic. Demand
can be inflated through low prices and lead, consequently, to an investment
program which is larger than can be justified purely on economic grounds.
In these circumstances, an acceptable program rate of return can mask
uneconomic projects. When this is a possibility, it is important to
supplement analysis of the development program as a whole with analyses of
specific project components of interest to ensure their returns do not fall
below the opportunity cost of capital. Such an analysis has been attempted
for the Koyna IV project component.
Program and Project Costs
11.      Capital and operating and maintenance costs of the 1989/90 to
1997/98 time-slice of the Western Region development program are shown in
Table 6. Capital costs cover investments in generation, transmission and
distribution. Generation investments comprise the least-cost development
program for the Western Region prepared by CIA for the 1987 National Power
Plan, updated to reflect changes in cost estimates and implementation
schedules. Investments in transmission and distribution, which are not
planned integrally with generation, have been included in an amount
equivalent to 60S of investments in generation. This allowance has been



-15 1-
Annex 5.1
Page 5 of 12
derived from the historic relationship between generation investments and
investments in transmission and distribution in the Western Region and from
the costs of projects included in CEA's recently completed National
Transmission Plan. All capital costs have been converted to economic terms
by removing taxes and duties and by revaluing the local component of costs
using an SCF of 0.8.
12.      Capital and iperating and maintenance costs for the separate
project analysis of Koyna IV, including the costs of investments in
associated transmission and distribution, are shown in Table 7.
Transmission investments are the costs of reinforcing the lines which
connect the existing Koyna station to bulk supply delivery points.
Distribution investments related to Koyna IV cannot be identified
separately from other investments in distribution in the Western Region.
Thus, an allowance has been included in proportion to the small amount of
incremental energy that will be provided by Koyna IV (47 GWh), in the same
way as was done for the program analysis. Capital costs have been shadow-
priced in the same way as for the program analysis.
13.      Incremental operating and maintenance costs have been estimated as
proportions of the capital costs of the power stations to which they
relate. For coal-fired and gas-fired stations, annual operating and
maintenance costs are estimated at 2.5X of station capital costs. For
hydroelectric stations, operations and maintenance costs are estimated at
1.132 of capital costs per annum. For transmission and distribution, costs
are estimated at 1.02 of capital costs per annum. Transmission and
distribution losses are estimated to amount to 152 of gross generation.
14.      In estimating incremental system fuel costs associated with the
program time-slice, the avera;e fuel consumption of new coal-fired power
stations has been taken as 0.61 kg per kWh of coal and 15 ml per kWh of oil
(used for stabilizing combustion). The economic cost of minemouth coal has
been estimated at Rs 193 per tonne and the cost of transporting coal at Rs
0.41 per ton km. The economic cost of fuel oil used in power stations has
been estimated at Rs 3.36 per liter (including transport). The opportunity
cost of natural gas to the power sector has been assessed as Rs 1,920 per
thousand m3 and the specific consumption of new gas-fired plant has been
assumed to be 0.23 m3 per kWh (an average of the consumptions of combined-
cycle and gas turbine plant).
Program Benefits
15.      Benefits that will be derived from the Western Region development
program relate mainly to the incremental electricity consumption that the
expansion in supplies will facilitate. In addition, the program may yield
benefits through reducing the costs of meeting existing levels of supply.
For example, investments in transmission and distribution in part are aimed
at reinforcing existing facilities to reduce power losses. Similarly, new
generation projects sometimes are able to reduce system fuel consumption
during off-peak periods by displacing less efficient existing plant from
operation (para 6). The development program may also provide benefits
through reducing the frequency of supply interruptions and stabilizing
supply voltages and frequencies, or through avoiding deteriorations in the



-152-
Annex 5.1
Page 6 of 12
quality of supplies that otherwise would have occurred. However, the
persistent shortages of generating capacity expected in the Western Region
through 1997198 (Table 1) suggest that by far the greater part of
additional capacity to be added will be used to expand supplies. Because
of this, possible fuel cost savings and quality of supply improvements have
not been taken into account in either the program or project analyses.
16.      Economic benefits from the expansion of supplies facilitated by
the development program (and its constituent projects) will be distributed
to electricity consumers throughout the Western Region. Partly, the extent
of these benefits can be measured by the additional revenues associated
with the expansion in consumption. However, the benefits consumers derive
from incremental consumption will be at least equal and probably above what
thc-: are required to pay for additional supplies, otherwise consumption
would not increase. The extent of consumers' net benefits (which are
referred to as consumers' surplus) can be estimated as savings consumers
obtain through access to public electricity compared with the costs of
private supplies. The program and project analyses have estimated rates of
return first with benefits limited to incremen*al revenues and then again
with allowance made for consumers' surplus. estimated benefits of the
program time-slice are summarized in Table 6. Estimated benefits of the
Koyna IV project component are summarized in Table 7.
Incremental Revenues
17.      Average tariff levele in the Western Region over the last few
years and their relationships to estimated marginal costs are shown in
Table 8. The average tariff level in 1987/88 (weighted by consumption
share) is estimated to have been 78 paise per kWh, equivalent to about 74Z
of marginal costs. The structure of states' tariffs is broadly similar
throughout the Region. Principal features of tariffs are their complexity,
the deep cross-subsidy of low voltage consumers (and agriculture in
particular) by industry, and the absence of any distinction between the
costs of supplying energy during peak and off-peak periods. The average of
tariffs to industrial consumers is equivalent to approximately 1152 of
marginal cost, whereas the average of agricultural tariffs is about only
10? of marginal cost. Tariffs to commercial consumers average about 60Z of
marginal cost and those to domestic consumers about 25Z of marginal cost.
In real terms, the average tariff level is estimated to have increased
14.8S between 1984/85 and 1987/88 (an average real increase of 4.7Z per
annum). However, there have been marked variations within the Region: in
Haharashtra the real increase over this period was 29.72 (HSEB's tariff
increased 25.0? in real terms - para 2.16), whereas the real increase was
only 7.41 in Hadhya Pradesh, and 3.0S in Gujarat. Industrial consumers and
purchasers of bulk supplies have borne the brunt of increases. Between
1984/85 and 1987/88, real tariffs to large industrial consumers increased
17.0? and real tariffs to bulk purchasers increased 34.0Z (MSEB's consumers
faced increases of 31.3Z and 34.9? respectively). In contrast, over this
period, real prices to agricultural consumers (15.4? of consumption)
dropped by half.



-153-
Annex 5.1
Page 7 of 12
18.      Future changes in average tariff levels will be the result both of
increases in prices and changing sectoral shares in consumption. As load
is expected to grow relatively more quickly amongst low voltage consumers
(para 3), the increase in share of consumption at low voltage will exert a
downwards pressure on average tariff level. However, GOI's 32 minimum rate
of return target places states under increased pressure to accelerate
tariff reform. This is most likely to materialize through greater
attention to load management. Faster rates of real tariff increase are
less likely, both because of tho sharp political constraints involved and
because of the diminishing difference between tariffs to industrial
consumers and the costs of private generation (Table 9). Estimations of
benefits from the proposed project have assumed that average tariff levels
in the Western Region over the next few years will increase in real terms
at only half their rate of real increase since 1984/85, that is 2.32 per
annum (para 17). Under this assumption, the average tariff level in
1989/90 (the start of the investment time-slice) would be 87 paise per kWh, 7/
equivalent to approximately 861 of marginal cost. By 1995/96 (when
Koyna IV would be commissioned), the average would be equivalent to 100
paise per kWh in constant 1989/90 prices. Industrial tariffs then would be
about equal marginal cost. It has therefore been assumed that after
1995/96, the average level will remain constant in real terms. Applying an
SCF of 0.8, the corresponding rates of economic benefit are estimated to be
70 paise per kWh in 1989190 using to 80 paise per kWh in l995/96.
Consumer Surplus
19.      In addition to the amounts paid for public supply, it har been
assumed that half of consumers would be willing to pay the additional costs
they would incur in meeting their electricity needs through private
generation. This assumption implies that consumers have a price elasticity
of minus unity. In practice, at low levels of consumption (as in India),
consumers are likely to be considerably less sensitive to price. This
suggests that more than half of consumers would be willing to pay the costs
of private supply and hence that consumer surplus is likely to accrue at
more than half of the additional costs of autogeneration.
20.      Estimated costs of autogeneration are summarized in Table 9.
Costs have been estimated separately for large diesel generators (in the
range 200 kW to 400 kW) as used by medium and large industries, and for
small generators (of about 50 kW capacity) as used in workshops and
commercial undertakings. Assuming 302 machine utilization (which is
typical for private generating installations), the average cost of
autogeneration is estimated at 125 paise per kWh from a large mwchine and
149 paise per kWh from a small machines.
21.      For agricultural consumers, the costs of replacing public
electricity have been estimated as the additional cost of providing
irrigation from diesel pumps. Table 10 illustrates the estimation.
Assuming pumps are operated for 800 hours per year (which seems typical),
electricity would have to be priced at 238 paise per kWh for diesel and
electric pumping to cost the same.
7/ The Bank estimates that India's domestic inflation in 1988/89 will be
9.02.



-154-
Annex 5.1
Page 8 of 12
22.      Table 11 su mmaarizes estimation of the average rate of consumer
surplus in the Western Region. The estimation assumes that for domestic
consumers, public lighting and comoercial consumers, the relevant
comparator is the generating cost of a small diesel set. For public water
pumping, industry and transport, the relevant comparator is assumed to be
the generating cost of a large diesel set. For agriculture, the comparator
is assumed to be the zost of diesel irrigation pumping. Using 1987/88
data, 8/ the weighted average rate at which consumer surplus is assumed to
accrue is 38 paise per kWh. Inflating to 1989/90 prices 9/ gives an
average rate of consumer surplus of 41 paise per kWh. Adding this to
expected average tariff levels brings total estimated economic benefits to
111 paise per kWh in 1989/90 rising to 121 paise per kWh in 1995/96.
Project Benefits
23.      The benefits of the Koyna IV project relate to the additional
value of concentrating the station's generation into periods when system
demands are highest. Also, through the project, the Koyna power station
will be able to generate an additional 47 GWh from the existir,g reservoir
because of the more efficient turbines to be installed in Koyna IV. At
present, only about one-third of Koyna's generation of 2,085 GWh per annum
coincides with peak demand periods. The remaining 1,390 GWh are provided
during off-peak periods. The benefits of Koyna IV therefore will be to
move 1,390 GWh of generation from off-peak to peak periods and to provide
an additional 47 GWh.
Incremental Revenues
24.      At present, there would be no financial benefit to the
concentration of generation into peak periods as retail tariffs in the
Western Region do not distinguish between the costs of supplying energy
during peak and off-peak periods. However, there is a strong likelihood
that two-part tariffs will be introduced for large consumers over the next
few years; as regards Maharashtra, this is one of the principal objectives
of the pricing component of the proposed project (para. 2.17). The
financial benefits of Koyna IV then will be the difference between peak and
off-peak revenues on Koyna's off-peak generation which would be
concentrated into peak periods.
25.      Table 2 suggests that in 1995/96 (when Koyna IV would be fully
commissioned) domestic, commercial and irrigation consumers will account
for approximately 40? of total consumption. Assuming modest progress with
load management initiatives (para 2.17), it is reasonable to assume that
the contribution of these consumers to peak system demands by 1995/96 would
be no more than 30Z of total consumption. Remaining consumers (principally
industry, traction and other bulk power purchases) would account for about
70? of consumption during peak periods. These other consumers could all be
S/ The most recent year for which tariff data are available by consumer
category.
9/ The Bank estimates that India's domestic inflation will be 9.0Z in
1- I

Page 9 of 12
supplied under two part tariffs. Hence, it is assumed that Koyna IV would
realize financial benefits on 702 of the generation moved to peak periods,
that in on approximately 975 GWh per annum and 848 GVh per annum net of
transmission and distribution losses.
26.      In 1987/88, tariffs to industry and other bulk purchasers averaged
96 paise per kWh (Table 11)   Real growth of 2.32 per annum (para 18) would
bring the tariff level to 12v paise per kWh in 1995196; close to the costs
of autogeneration. It is reasonable to assume that the average tariff
level for industry could not exceed the average cost of autogeneration.
Also, that the peak and off-peak rates of a two-part tariff would have to
be set in relation to the fixed and variable costs of autogeneration to
avoid uneconomic supply switching. 10/ Taking a large industrial generator
as a benchmark, and considering a daily peak of three hours duration
(typical for the Westein Region), the peak energy rate of a two-part tariff
for 1995/96 should not exceed 180 paise per kWh (Table 4). For an average
tariff level of 120 paise per kWh, the corresponding off-peak rate would be
80 paise per kWh. Concentrating Koyna's generation into periods of peak
demand therefore is assumed to yield incremental revenue of 100 paise per
kWh, or approximately Rs 848 million per annum on the generation moved into
peak periods. The economic value of this would be Rs 678 million per annum
(applying an SCF of 0.8).
27.      In addition to these benefits, Koyna IV will generate incremental
revenues on the 47 GWh per annum of additional generation the station will
provide. Net of transmission and distribution losses, the economic value
of this peak generation would be Rs 59 million per annum, bringing the
total value of incremental revenues to Rs 737 million per annum.
Consumer Surplus
28.      In addition to the incremental revenues the project will generate.
Koyna IV will provide additional benefits to consumers (consumer surplus)
through expanding supplies in peak periods, when consumers' willingness-to-
pay is highest. However, by 1995/96, the level of tariffs to indl:stry and
other large consumers would be close to the costs of autogeneration
(para 26). Because of this, the full benefits to these consumers largely
would be captured through incremental revenues. The additional consumer
surplus yielded by Koyna IV consequently would essentially be confined to
domestic, commercial and agricultural const3mers, that is to only
approximately 301 of the generation moved to peak periods (415 GWh,
para 25).
29.      By 1995/96, the (weighted) average tariff to domestic, commercial
and agricultural consumers would be 70 paise per kWh (assuming real
increases of 2.32 per annum, para 26). It is estimated that average
autogeneration costs to domestic and commercial consumers are 149 paise per
kWh and that diesel irrigation pumping costs the equivalent of 238 paise
per kWh (Table 10). Dividing these costs between peak and off-peak

generator that is made financially attractive.
