Page 10 of 12
periods, suggests peak autogeneration costs of 215 paise per kWh and diesel
pumping costs during periods of peak demand equivalent to 343 paise per
kWh. 11/ The average of these costs is 279 paise per kWh, suggesting a
rate of consumer surplus to low voltage consumers during peak demand
periods of 209 paise per kWh (i.e. 279-70 paise per kWh). To estimate the
additional consumer surplus attributable to Koyna IV, the surplus of 209
paise per kWh must be reduced by the surplus that would have accrued to the
415 GWh per annur or generation in question in the event of it continuing
to be supplied during off-peak periods. The variable costs of
autogeneration and diesel pumping (Tables 9 and 10) suggest a rate of
consumer surplus to low voltage consumers during off-peak periods of 60
paise per kWh (130-70 paise per kWh). It is again assumed that only half
of consumers would be prepared to pay the higher costs of autogeneration
and diesel pumping (para 19). Hence the average rate of consumer surplus
to low voltage consumers during off-peak periods is estimated to be
30 paise per kWh. This suggests that the additional consumer surplus Koyna
IV will yield to low voltage consumers will be about 180 paise per kWh
(i.e. 209-30 paise per kWh), equivalent to US Cents 12 per kWh.
30.      It must be remembered that this rate of surplus applies only to
consumption by low voltage consumers, and so to only 360 GWh of Koyna's
total net generation of 1815 GWh (2085 GWh divided by 1.15); that is to
only 302 of the two-thirds of Koyna's generation being concentrated into
peak periods (para 28). The total value of this surplus would be Rs 648
million per annum. The comparatively high rate of surplus is a reflection
that none of the benefits of Koyna IV to low voltage consumers are expected
to be captared through incremental revenues; it having been assumed that it
will not be practicable to introduce two-part tariffs for these consumers
(para 25).
Economic Rate of Return
Program Aralysis
31.      The estimated rates of return of the 1989/90 to 1997/98 time-slice
of the Western Region development program are:
(i) 7.2Z counting as program benefits only incremental revenues
generated from rrojects included in the time-slice; and
(ii) 14.0? when allowance is made for associated consumer surplus.
It must be noted that the above estimations are based only on the estimated
values of incremental consumption. In practice, the development program
will yield additional benefits through improving the quality of electricity
supplies to existing consumers (para 17).  With a 12Z discount factor, the
11/ Peak autogeneration and diesel pumping costs have been estimated as the
fixed costs of autogeneration and diesel pumping divided over peak
demand periods of 3 hours per day (para 26 and Tables 9 and 10) plus
variable operating costs.



-157-
Annex 5.1
Page il of 12
NPV of the time-slice (including the allowance for consumer surplus) is
estimated at Rs 12,294 millions (equivalent to US$820 millions).
32.      The analysis suggests that the program time-slice cannot be
justified purely on the basis of incremental revenues, but that the
economic viability of the program is sound, considering the associated
consumer surplus. As retail power tariffs in the Western Region on average
are well below marginal cost (para 16), 4t is not surprising that
incremental revenues alone do not yield returns comparable with the
opportunity cost of capital. Rather, the low return is more a reflection
of the distribution of net benefits of the program time-slice between power
utilities in the Western Region and their consumers. The above assumptions
regarding the rate at which consumer surplus accrues suggest that roughly
661 of benefits would be realised by power utilities and 34Z by electricity
consumers.
33.      Sensitivity analyses of the program time-slice are suummarized in
Tables 12 and 13. Adverse changes in program costs and/or the rates of
benefits amounting (in total) to more than 102 would reduce the rate of
return to less than 12Z. In these circumstances, some reconfiguration of
projects would be needed to ensure that the development program remained
economic. (Economic in the sense of earning a return in excess of the
opportunity cost of capital). Similarly, the sensitivity analyses show
that a delay in implementing projects included in the time-slice of more
than one year would also reduce economic returns to less than 122 and
require adjustment of the program. As the cost and benefit variations
which the program can withstand are relatively modest, it is not unlikely
that some adjustments of the program may be required as implementation
proceeds. The project analysis which follows demonstrates that the in such
an eventuality, Koyna IV would not be a preferred cand.idate for deferral.
It must be remembered, of course, that set against these adverse
sensitivities are equal likelihoods of more favorable outcomes.
Project Analysis
34.      The estimated rates of return of the Koyna IV component of the
proposed project are:
(i) 9.51 counting as benefits only incremental revenues; and
(ii) 18.32 when allowance is made for the associated consumer
surplus.
Estimations again are based on benefits confined to the value of
incremental consumption. With a 122 discount factor, the NPV of Koyna IV
is estimated at Rs 2,371 millions (equivalent to US$ 158 ;'illions).
35.      Sensitivity analyses of the project are summarized in Tables 14
and 15. The analyses suggest that returns yielded by Koyna IV are more
robust than those of the development program as a whole. For example,
including the allowance for consumer surplus, Koyna IV could absorb adverse
changes in costs and/or benefits of about 40? (in total) and the project



-158-
Annex 5.1
Page 12 of 12
would remain economic. Similarly, the analyses show that the project could
remain economic under implementation delays exceeding three years: how long
in practice would depend on the extent of associated cost increases.
These results suggest that, in the event that some adjustment of the
Western Region program became desirable, deferral of other projects in the
program would provide more relief than deferral of Koyna IV. Variations of
costs and benefits exceeding 40 are considered very unlikely. Similarly,
implementation delays exceeding three years are considered very unlikely.
Hence there is a strong likelihood that Koyna IV will prove economic.
